border-bottom: 1px solid #aaa;">\n<a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_English_history" title="Timeline of English history">Timeline</a></td></tr><tr><td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Periods_in_English_history" title="Template:Periods in English history"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Periods_in_English_history" title="Template talk:Periods in English history"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Periods_in_English_history&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_of_Great_Britain_and_Ireland" title="United Kingdom of Great Britain and Ireland">United Kingdom of Great Britain and Ireland</a> was one of the <a href="/wiki/Allies_of_World_War_I" title="Allies of World War I">Allied Powers</a> during the First World War of 1914\xe2\x80\x931918, fighting against the <a href="/wiki/Central_Powers" title="Central Powers">Central Powers</a> (the <a href="/wiki/German_Empire" title="German Empire">German Empire</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Austro-Hungarian_Empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Austro-Hungarian Empire">Austro-Hungarian Empire</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a><sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Bulgaria" title="Kingdom of Bulgaria">Kingdom of Bulgaria</a><sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>). The state\'s armed forces were reorganised\xe2\x80\x94the war marked the founding of the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Air_Force" title="Royal Air Force">Royal Air Force</a>, for example\xe2\x80\x94and increased in size because of the introduction, in January 1916, of <a href="/wiki/Conscription" title="Conscription">conscription</a> for the first time in the country\'s history as well as the raising of what was, at the time, the largest all-volunteer army in history, known as <a href="/wiki/Kitchener%27s_Army" title="Kitchener&#39;s Army">Kitchener\'s Army</a>, of more than 2,000,000 men.<sup id="cite_ref-TuckerRobertsEnc_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TuckerRobertsEnc-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>504</span></sup> The outbreak of war has generally been regarded as a socially unifying event,<sup id="cite_ref-social_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-social-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> although this view has been challenged by more recent scholarship. In any case, responses in Great Britain in 1914 were similar to those amongst populations across Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On the eve of war, there was serious domestic unrest in the UK (amongst the labour and suffrage movements and especially in Ireland) but much of the population rapidly rallied behind the government. Significant sacrifices were made in the name of defeating the Empire\'s enemies and many of those who could not fight contributed to philanthropic and humanitarian causes. Fearing food shortages and labour shortfalls, the government passed legislation such as the <a href="/wiki/Defence_of_the_Realm_Act_1914" title="Defence of the Realm Act 1914">Defence of the Realm Act 1914</a>, to give it new powers. The war saw a move away from the idea of "<a href="/wiki/Business_as_usual_(policy)" title="Business as usual (policy)">business as usual</a>" under <a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Prime Minister of the United Kingdom">Prime Minister</a> <a href="/wiki/H._H._Asquith" title="H. H. Asquith">H. H. Asquith</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Baker_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Baker-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> and towards a state of <a href="/wiki/Total_war" title="Total war">total war</a> (complete state intervention in public affairs) under the premiership of <a href="/wiki/David_Lloyd_George" title="David Lloyd George">David Lloyd George</a>;<sup id="cite_ref-totalwar_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-totalwar-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> the first time this had been seen in Britain. The war also witnessed the first <a href="/wiki/German_strategic_bombing_during_World_War_I" title="German strategic bombing during World War I">aerial bombardments of cities in Britain</a>.\n</p><p>Newspapers played an important role in maintaining popular support for the war.<sup id="cite_ref-beckett394_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-beckett394-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> Large quantities of propaganda were produced by the government under the guidance of such journalists as <a href="/wiki/Charles_Masterman" title="Charles Masterman">Charles Masterman</a> and newspaper owners such as <a href="/wiki/Max_Aitken,_1st_Baron_Beaverbrook" title="Max Aitken, 1st Baron Beaverbrook">Lord Beaverbrook</a>. By adapting to the changing demographics of the workforce (or the "dilution of labour", as it was termed), war-related industries grew rapidly, and production increased, as concessions were quickly made to <a href="/wiki/Trade_union" title="Trade union">trade unions</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-beckett341_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-beckett341-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> In that regard, the war is also credited by some with drawing women into mainstream employment for the first time.<sup id="cite_ref-beckett455_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-beckett455-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> Debates continue about the impact the war had on women\'s emancipation, given that a large number of women were granted the vote for the first time in <a href="/wiki/1918_UK_general_election" class="mw-redirect" title="1918 UK general election">1918</a>. The experience of individual women during the war varied; much depended on locality, age, marital status and occupation.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The civilian death rate rose due to food shortages and <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Flu" class="mw-redirect" title="Spanish Flu">Spanish Flu</a>, which hit the country in 1918.<sup id="cite_ref-WAR_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WAR-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> Military deaths are estimated to have exceeded 850,000.<sup id="cite_ref-Mitchell_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mitchell-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>  The Empire reached its zenith at the conclusion of peace negotiations.<sup id="cite_ref-Beckett564_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Beckett564-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> However, the war heightened not only imperial loyalties but also individual national identities in the Dominions (Canada, Newfoundland, Australia, New Zealand and South Africa) and India. <a href="/wiki/Irish_nationalists" class="mw-redirect" title="Irish nationalists">Irish nationalists</a> after 1916 moved from collaboration with London to demands for immediate independence (see <a href="/wiki/Easter_Rising" title="Easter Rising">Easter Rising</a>), a move given great impetus by the <a href="/wiki/Conscription_Crisis_of_1918" title="Conscription Crisis of 1918">Conscription Crisis of 1918</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-pierce_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pierce-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Military historians continue to debate matters of tactics and strategy. However, in terms of memory of the war, historian Adrian Gregory argues that:\n</p>\n<dl><dd>"The verdict of popular culture is more or less unanimous. The First World War was stupid, tragic and futile. The stupidity of the war has been a theme of growing strength since the 1920s. From <a href="/wiki/Robert_Graves" title="Robert Graves">Robert Graves</a>, through \'<a href="/wiki/Oh!_What_a_Lovely_War" title="Oh! What a Lovely War">Oh! What a Lovely War</a>\' to \'<a href="/wiki/Blackadder_Goes_Forth" title="Blackadder Goes Forth">Blackadder Goes Forth</a>,\' the criminal idiocy of the British High Command has become an article of faith."<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup></dd></dl>\n<div id="toc" class="toc"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" style="display:none" /><div class="toctitle" lang="en" dir="ltr"><h2>Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" class="toclevel-1 tocsection-37"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">15</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-38"><a class="tocnumber">17</span> <span class="toctext">Further reading</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-40"><a href="#Surveys"><span class="tocnumber">17.1</span> <span class="toctext">Surveys</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-41"><a href="#Politics"><span class="tocnumber">17.2</span> <span class="toctext">Politics</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-42"><a href="#Empire"><span class="tocnumber">17.3</span> <span class="toctext">Empire</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-43"><a href="#Economics"><span class="tocnumber">17.4</span> <span class="toctext">Economics</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-44"><a href="#Propaganda_and_popular_culture"><span class="tocnumber">17.5</span> <span class="toctext">Propaganda and popular culture</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-45"><a href="#Posters"><span class="tocnumber">17.5.1</span> <span class="toctext">Posters</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-46"><a href="#Year_books"><span class="tocnumber">17.6</span> <span class="toctext">Year books</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-47"><a href="#Women,_family_and_society"><span class="tocnumber">17.7</span> <span class="toctext">Women, family and society</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-48"><a href="#Primary_sources"><span class="tocnumber">17.8</span> <span class="toctext">Primary sources</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-49"><a href="#Historiography_and_memory"><span class="tocnumber">17.9</span> <span class="toctext">Historiography and memory</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Government">Government</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_the_United_Kingdom_during_the_First_World_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Government">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Causes_of_World_War_I" title="Causes of World War I">Causes of World War I</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Herbert_Henry_Asquith.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Studio portrait of older man, very well dressed with determined expression" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Herbert_Henry_Asquith.jpg/220px-Herbert_Henry_Asquith.jpg" width="220" height="288" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Herbert_Henry_Asquith.jpg/330px-Herbert_Henry_Asquith.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Herbert_Henry_Asquith.jpg/440px-Herbert_Henry_Asquith.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2000" data-file-height="2615" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Herbert_Henry_Asquith.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/H._H._Asquith" title="H. H. Asquith">H. H. Asquith</a> (c. 1915), Prime Minister at the beginning of the war</div></div></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Asquith_as_Prime_Minister">Asquith as Prime Minister</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_the_United_Kingdom_during_the_First_World_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Asquith as Prime Minister">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Asquith_coalition_ministry" title="Asquith coalition ministry">Asquith coalition ministry</a></div>\n<p>On 4 August, the King declared war on the advice of H. H. Asquith of the <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Party_(UK)" title="Liberal Party (UK)">Liberal Party</a> Prime Minister. Britain\'s basic reasons for declaring war focused on a deep commitment to France and avoidance of splitting the Liberal Party. Top Liberals threatened to resign if the cabinet refused to support France\xe2\x80\x94which would mean loss of control of the government to a coalition or to the Unionist (ie Conservative) opposition. \nHowever, the large antiwar element among Liberals would support the war to honour the 1839 treaty regarding guarantees of Belgian neutrality, so that rather than France was the public reason given.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Scrap_of_Paper_-_Enlist_Today.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/The_Scrap_of_Paper_-_Enlist_Today.jpg/240px-The_Scrap_of_Paper_-_Enlist_Today.jpg" width="240" height="365" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/The_Scrap_of_Paper_-_Enlist_Today.jpg/360px-The_Scrap_of_Paper_-_Enlist_Today.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/The_Scrap_of_Paper_-_Enlist_Today.jpg/480px-The_Scrap_of_Paper_-_Enlist_Today.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1842" data-file-height="2800" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Scrap_of_Paper_-_Enlist_Today.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>"The Scrap of Paper \xe2\x80\x93 Enlist Today", 1914 British propaganda poster emphasizes defence of Belgium.</div></div></div>\n<p>Therefore the public reason given out by the government. and used in posters, was that Britain was required to safeguard Belgium\'s neutrality under the 1839 <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_London_(1839)" title="Treaty of London (1839)">Treaty of London</a>.\n</p><p>The strategic risk posed by German control of the Belgian and ultimately French coast was considered unacceptable. German guarantees of post-war behaviour were cast into doubt by her blas\xc3\xa9 treatment of Belgian neutrality. However, the Treaty of London had not committed Britain on her own to safeguard Belgium\'s neutrality. Moreover, naval war planning demonstrated that Britain herself would have violated Belgian neutrality by blockading her ports (to prevent imported goods passing to Germany) in the event of war with Germany.\n</p><p>Britain\'s duty to her Entente partners, both France and Russia, were paramount factors. The Foreign Secretary <a href="/wiki/Edward_Grey,_1st_Viscount_Grey_of_Fallodon" title="Edward Grey, 1st Viscount Grey of Fallodon">Edward Grey</a> argued that the secret naval agreements with France  created a moral obligation \'to save France from defeat by Germany.  British national interest rejected German control of France, Belgium and the Netherlands. Grey warned that to abandon its allies would be a permanent disaster: if Germany won the war, or the Entente won without British support, then, either way, Britain would be left without any friends. This would have left both Britain and her Empire vulnerable to isolation.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Eyre_Crowe" title="Eyre Crowe">Eyre Crowe</a>, a senior Foreign office expert  said:\n</p>\n<blockquote>\n<p>"Should the war come, and England stand aside, one of two things must happen. (a) Either Germany and Austria win, crush France and humiliate Russia. What will be the position of a friendless England? (b) Or France and Russia win. What would be their attitude towards England? What about India and the Mediterranean?"<sup id="cite_ref-ClarkSleepwalkers_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ClarkSleepwalkers-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>544</span></sup>\n</p>\n</blockquote>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Crisis_of_Liberal_leadership">Crisis of Liberal leadership</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_the_United_Kingdom_during_the_First_World_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Crisis of Liberal leadership">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The Liberal Party might have survived a short war, but the totality of the Great War called for strong measures that the Party had long rejected.  The result was the permanent destruction of the ability of the Liberal Party to lead a government. Historian <a href="/wiki/Robert_Blake,_Baron_Blake" title="Robert Blake, Baron Blake">Robert Blake</a> explains the dilemma:\n</p>\n<dl><dd>the Liberals were traditionally the party of freedom of speech, conscience and trade.  They were against jingoism, heavy armaments and compulsion....Liberals were neither wholehearted nor unanimous about conscription, censorship,  the <a href="/wiki/Defence_of_the_Realm_Act" class="mw-redirect" title="Defence of the Realm Act">Defence of the Realm Act</a>, severity toward aliens and pacifists, direction of labour and industry.  The Conservatives... had no such misgivings.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup></dd></dl>\n<p>Blake further notes that it was the Liberals, not the Conservatives who needed the moral outrage of Belgium to justify going to war, while the Conservatives called for intervention from the start of the crisis on the grounds of <i><a href="/wiki/Realpolitik" title="Realpolitik">realpolitik</a></i> and the balance of power.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The British people were disappointed that there was no quick victory in the war. They long had taken great great pride and expense in the Royal Navy, but now there was little to cheer about. The <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Jutland" title="Battle of Jutland">Battle of Jutland</a> in May 1916, was the first and only time the German fleet challenged control of the North Sea, but it was overmatched and was reassigned mostly to helping the more important u-boats.  Since the Liberals ran the war without consulting the Unionists (Conservatives) there were heavy partisan attacks.  However even Liberal commentators were dismayed by the lack of energy at the top. At the time public opinion was intensely hostile, both in the media and in the street, against any young man in civilian garb and labeled as a slacker. The leading Liberal newspaper, the <i>Manchester Guardian</i> complained&#160;:\n</p>\n<dl><dd>The fact that the Government has not dared to challenge the nation to rise above itself, is one among many signs... The war is, in fact, not being taken seriously.... How can any slacker be blamed when the Government itself is slack.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup></dd></dl>\n<p>Asquith\'s Liberal government was brought down in <span class="nowrap">May 1915</span>, due in particular to a <a href="/wiki/Shell_Crisis_of_1915" title="Shell Crisis of 1915">crisis in inadequate artillery shell production</a> and the protest resignation of <a href="/wiki/John_Fisher,_1st_Baron_Fisher" title="John Fisher, 1st Baron Fisher">Admiral Fisher</a> over the disastrous  <a href="/wiki/Gallipoli_Campaign" title="Gallipoli Campaign">Gallipoli Campaign</a> against Turkey. Reluctant to face doom in an election, Asquith formed a new coalition government on <span class="nowrap">25 May</span>, with the majority of the new cabinet coming from his own Liberal party and the <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Party_(UK)" title="Conservative Party (UK)">Unionist (Conservative) party</a>, along with a token Labour representation. The new government lasted a year and a half, and was the last time Liberals controlled the government.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> The analysis of historian <a href="/wiki/A._J._P._Taylor" title="A. J. P. Taylor">A. J. P. Taylor</a> is that the British people were so deeply divided over numerous issues, But on all sides there was growing distrust for the Asquith government. There was no agreement whatsoever on wartime issues. The leaders of the two parties realized that embittered debates in Parliament would further undermine  popular morale, and so the House of Commons did not once discuss the war before May 1915. Taylor argues:<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> \n</p>\n<dl><dd>The Unionists, by and large, regarded Germany as a dangerous rival, and rejoiced at the chance to destroy her. They meant to fight a hard-headed war by ruthless methods; the condemned Liberal \'softness\' before the war and now. The Liberals insisted on remaining high-minded. Many of them income to support the war only when the Germans invaded Belgium....Entering the war for idealistic motives, the Liberals wish to fight it by noble means and found it harder to abandon their principles than to endure your defeat in the field.</dd></dl>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Lloyd_George_as_Prime_Minister">Lloyd George as Prime Minister</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_the_United_Kingdom_during_the_First_World_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Lloyd George as Prime Minister">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Lloyd_George_ministry" title="Lloyd George ministry">Lloyd George ministry</a></div>\n<p>This coalition government lasted until 1916, when the Unionists became dissatisfied with Asquith and the Liberals\' conduct of affairs, particularly over the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Somme" title="Battle of the Somme">Battle of the Somme</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-mcgill_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mcgill-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>  Asquith\'s opponents now took control, led by  <a href="/wiki/Bonar_Law" title="Bonar Law">Bonar Law</a> (leader of the Conservatives), <a href="/wiki/Sir_Edward_Carson" class="mw-redirect" title="Sir Edward Carson">Sir Edward Carson</a> (leader of the <a href="/wiki/Ulster_Unionists" class="mw-redirect" title="Ulster Unionists">Ulster Unionists</a>), and <a href="/wiki/David_Lloyd_George" title="David Lloyd George">David Lloyd George</a> (then a minister in the cabinet). Law, who had few allies outside his own party, lacked sufficient support to form a new coalition; the Liberal Lloyd George, on the other hand, enjoyed much wider support and duly formed a majority-Conservative <a href="/wiki/Coalition_Government_1916%E2%80%931922" class="mw-redirect" title="Coalition Government 1916\xe2\x80\x931922">coalition government</a> with Lloyd George Liberals and Labour. Asquith was still the party head but he and his followers moved to the opposition benches in Parliament.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Lloyd George immediately set about transforming the British war effort, taking firm control of both military and domestic policy.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> In the first 235 days of its existence, the War Cabinet met 200 times.<sup id="cite_ref-totalwar_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-totalwar-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> Its creation marked the transition to a state of <a href="/wiki/Total_war" title="Total war">total war</a>\xe2\x80\x94the idea that every man, woman and child should play his or her part in the war effort. Moreover, it was decided that members of the government should be the men who controlled the war effort, primarily utilising the power they had been given under the <a href="/wiki/Defence_of_the_Realm_Act" class="mw-redirect" title="Defence of the Realm Act">Defence of the Realm Act</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-totalwar_9-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-totalwar-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> For the first time, the government could react quickly, without endless bureaucracy to tie it down, and with up-to-date statistics on such matters as the state of the <a href="/wiki/Merchant_navy" title="Merchant navy">merchant navy</a> and farm production.<sup id="cite_ref-totalwar_9-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-totalwar-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> The policy marked a distinct shift away from Asquith\'s initial policy of <a href="/wiki/Laissez-faire" title="Laissez-faire">laissez-faire</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Baker_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Baker-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> which had been characterised by <a href="/wiki/Winston_Churchill" title="Winston Churchill">Winston Churchill</a>\'s declaration of "<a href="/wiki/Business_as_usual_(policy)" title="Business as usual (policy)">business as usual</a>" in <span class="nowrap">November 1914</span>.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> The success of Lloyd George\'s government can also be attributed to a general lack of desire for an election, and the practical absence of dissent that this brought about.<sup id="cite_ref-beckett499_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-beckett499-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:LloydGeorge.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Head and shoulders of a middle-aged gentleman with a bushy moustache" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/LloydGeorge.jpg/220px-LloydGeorge.jpg" width="220" height="277" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/LloydGeorge.jpg/330px-LloydGeorge.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/LloydGeorge.jpg/440px-LloydGeorge.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3500" data-file-height="4406" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:LloydGeorge.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>David Lloyd George (c. 1920), prime minister at the end of the war</div></div></div><p> In rapid succession in spring 1918 came a series of military and political crises.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> The Germans, having moved troops from the Eastern front and retrained them in new tactics, now had more soldiers on the Western Front than the Allies. On 21 March 1918 Germany launched a full scale <a href="/wiki/Spring_Offensive" title="Spring Offensive">Spring Offensive</a> against the British and French lines, hoping for victory on the battlefield before United States troops arrived in large numbers. The Allied armies fell back 40 miles in confusion, and facing defeat London realized it needed more troops to fight a mobile war. Lloyd George found half a million soldiers and rushed them to France, asked American President <a href="/wiki/Woodrow_Wilson" title="Woodrow Wilson">Woodrow Wilson</a> for immediate help, and agreed to the appointment of the French Marshal Foch as commander in chief on the Western Front, so that Allied forces could be coordinated to handle the German offensive.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Despite strong warnings that it was a bad idea, the War Cabinet <a href="/wiki/Conscription_Crisis_of_1918" title="Conscription Crisis of 1918">decided to impose conscription on Ireland</a> in 1918. The main reason was that labour in Britain demanded it as the price for cutting back on exemptions for certain workers. Labour wanted the principle established that no one was exempt, but it did not demand that conscription should actually take place in Ireland. The proposal was enacted, but never enforced. The Roman Catholic bishops for the first time entered the fray, calling for open resistance to compulsory military service, while the majority of Irish nationalists moved to supporting the intransigent <a href="/wiki/Sinn_F%C3%A9in" title="Sinn F\xc3\xa9in">Sinn F\xc3\xa9in</a> movement (away from the constitutional <a href="/wiki/Irish_National_Party" class="mw-redirect" title="Irish National Party">Irish National Party</a>). This proved a decisive moment, marking the end of Irish willingness to stay inside the Union.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On <span class="nowrap">7 May 1918</span>, a senior army officer on active duty, Major-General <a href="/wiki/Frederick_Barton_Maurice" class="mw-redirect" title="Frederick Barton Maurice">Sir Frederick Maurice</a>, prompted <a href="/wiki/Maurice_Debate" title="Maurice Debate">a second crisis</a> when he went public with allegations that Lloyd George had lied to Parliament on military matters. Asquith, the Liberal leader in the House, took up the allegations and attacked Lloyd George (also a Liberal). While Asquith\'s presentation was poor, Lloyd George vigorously defended his position, treating the debate as a vote of confidence. He won over the House with a powerful refutation of Maurice\'s allegations. The main results were to strengthen Lloyd George, weaken Asquith, end public criticism of overall strategy, and strengthen civilian control of the military.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> Meanwhile, the German offensive stalled and was ultimately reversed. Victory came on 11 November 1918.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Historian George H. Cassar has evaluated Lloyd George\'s legacy as a war leader:\n</p>\n<dl><dd>After all that has been said and done, what are we to make of Lloyd George\xe2\x80\x99s legacy as a war leader? On the home front he achieved varied results in tackling difficult, and in some instances, unprecedented problems. It would be hard to have improved on his dealings with labour and the program to increase homegrown food, but in the sectors of manpower, price control and food distribution he adopted the same approach as his predecessor, taking action only in response to the changing nature of the conflict. In the vital area of national morale, while he did not have the technical advantages of Churchill, his personal conduct damaged his ability to do more to inspire the nation. All things considered, it is unlikely that any of his political contemporaries could have handled matters at home as effectively as he did, although it can be argued that if someone else had been in charge, the difference would not have been sufficient to change the final outcome. In his conduct of the war he did advance the cause of the Entente significantly in some ways, but in determining strategy, one of the most important tasks for which a prime minister must be responsible, he was undeniably a failure. To sum up, while Lord George\'s contributions outweighed his mistakes, the margin is too narrow, in my opinion, to include him In the pantheon of Britain\'s outstanding war leaders.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup></dd></dl>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Collapse_of_the_Liberal_Party">Collapse of the Liberal Party</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_the_United_Kingdom_during_the_First_World_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Collapse of the Liberal Party">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>In the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1918" title="United Kingdom general election, 1918">general election of 1918</a>, Lloyd George, "the Man Who Won the War", led his coalition into another <i><a href="/wiki/Khaki_election" title="Khaki election">khaki election</a></i> and won a sweeping victory over the Asquithian Liberals and the newly emerging <a href="/wiki/Labour_Party_(UK)" title="Labour Party (UK)">Labour Party</a>. Lloyd George and the Conservative leader <a href="/wiki/Bonar_Law" title="Bonar Law">Bonar Law</a> wrote a joint letter of support to candidates to indicate they were considered the official Coalition candidates \xe2\x80\x93 this <a href="/wiki/The_Coalition_Coupon" class="mw-redirect" title="The Coalition Coupon">"coupon"</a>, as it became known, was issued to opponents of many sitting Liberal MPs, devastating the incumbents.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> Asquith and most of his Liberal colleagues lost their seats. Lloyd George was increasingly under the influence of the rejuvenated Conservative party. The Liberal party never recovered.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Finance">Finance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_the_United_Kingdom_during_the_First_World_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Finance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Before the war, the government spent 13 percent of <a href="/wiki/Gross_national_product" class="mw-redirect" title="Gross national product">gross national product</a> (GNP); in 1918 it spent 59 percent of GNP. The war was financed by borrowing large sums at home and abroad, by new taxes, and by inflation. It was implicitly financed by postponing maintenance and repair, and cancelling unneeded projects.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> The government avoided indirect taxes because they raised the cost of living, and caused discontent among the working class. In 1913\xe2\x80\x9314, indirect taxes on tobacco and alcohol yielded \xc2\xa375 million, while direct taxes yielded \xc2\xa388 million, including an income tax of \xc2\xa344 million and estate duties of \xc2\xa322 million. That is, 54 percent of revenue came from direct taxes; by 1918, direct taxes were 80 percent of revenue.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> There was a strong emphasis on being "fair" and being "scientific." The public generally supported the heavy new taxes, with minimal complaints. The Treasury rejected proposals for a stiff capital levy, which the Labour Party wanted to use to weaken the capitalists. Instead, there was an excess profits tax, of 50 percent of profits above the normal prewar level; the rate was raised to 80 percent in 1917.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup>  Excise taxes were added on luxury imports such as automobiles, clocks and watches. There was no sales tax or value added tax. The main increase in revenue came from income tax, which in 1915 went up to 3s. 6d in the pound (17.5%), and individual exemptions were lowered. The income tax rate grew to 5s in the pound (25%) in 1916, and 6s (30%) in 1918. Altogether, taxes provided at most 30 percent of national expenditure, with the rest from borrowing. The national debt soared from \xc2\xa3625 million to \xc2\xa37,800 million. Government bonds typically paid five percent. Inflation escalated so that the pound in 1919 purchased only a third of the basket it had purchased it 1914. Wages were laggard, and the poor and retired were especially hard hit.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Monarchy">Monarchy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_the_United_Kingdom_during_the_First_World_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Monarchy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:A_Good_Riddance_-_George_V_of_the_United_Kingdom_cartoon_in_Punch,_1917.png" class="image"><img alt="A man, wearing a crown and holding a broom, sweeps away a collection of other crowns" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/A_Good_Riddance_-_George_V_of_the_United_Kingdom_cartoon_in_Punch%2C_1917.png/220px-A_Good_Riddance_-_George_V_of_the_United_Kingdom_cartoon_in_Punch%2C_1917.png" width="220" height="282" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/A_Good_Riddance_-_George_V_of_the_United_Kingdom_cartoon_in_Punch%2C_1917.png/330px-A_Good_Riddance_-_George_V_of_the_United_Kingdom_cartoon_in_Punch%2C_1917.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/A_Good_Riddance_-_George_V_of_the_United_Kingdom_cartoon_in_Punch%2C_1917.png/440px-A_Good_Riddance_-_George_V_of_the_United_Kingdom_cartoon_in_Punch%2C_1917.png 2x" data-file-width="1356" data-file-height="1736" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:A_Good_Riddance_-_George_V_of_the_United_Kingdom_cartoon_in_Punch,_1917.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>"A good riddance"<br />A 1917 <i><a href="/wiki/Punch_(magazine)" title="Punch (magazine)">Punch</a></i> cartoon depicts King George sweeping away his German titles.</div></div></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/George_V#First_World_War" title="George V">George V \xc2\xa7&#160;First World War</a></div>\n<p>The British Royal Family faced a serious problem during the First World War because of its blood ties to the ruling family of Germany, Britain\'s prime adversary in the war. Before the war, the British royal family had been known as the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Saxe-Coburg_and_Gotha" title="House of Saxe-Coburg and Gotha">House of Saxe-Coburg and Gotha</a>. In 1910, <a href="/wiki/George_V" title="George V">George V</a> became King on the death of his father, <a href="/wiki/Edward_VII" title="Edward VII">King Edward VII</a>, and remained king throughout the war. He was the <a href="/wiki/Cousin_chart" class="mw-redirect" title="Cousin chart">first cousin</a> of the German Kaiser <a href="/wiki/William_II,_German_Emperor" class="mw-redirect" title="William II, German Emperor">Wilhelm&#160;II</a>, who came to symbolise all the horrors of the war. <a href="/wiki/Mary_of_Teck" title="Mary of Teck">Queen Mary</a>, although British like her mother, was the daughter of the <a href="/wiki/Duke_of_Teck" title="Duke of Teck">Duke of Teck</a>, a descendant of the German <a href="/wiki/Rulers_of_W%C3%BCrttemberg" class="mw-redirect" title="Rulers of W\xc3\xbcrttemberg">Royal House of W\xc3\xbcrttemberg</a>. During the war <a href="/wiki/H._G._Wells" title="H. G. Wells">H. G. Wells</a> wrote about Britain\'s "alien and uninspiring court", and George famously replied: "I may be uninspiring, but I\'ll be damned if I\'m alien."<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On 17 July 1917, to appease British nationalist feelings, King George issued an <a href="/wiki/Order_in_Council" title="Order in Council">Order in Council</a> that changed the name of the <a href="/wiki/British_Royal_Family" class="mw-redirect" title="British Royal Family">British Royal Family</a> to the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Windsor" title="House of Windsor">House of Windsor</a>. He specifically adopted Windsor as the surname for all descendants of <a href="/wiki/Victoria_of_the_United_Kingdom" class="mw-redirect" title="Victoria of the United Kingdom">Queen Victoria</a> then living in Britain, excluding women who married into other families and their descendants.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> He and his relatives who were British subjects relinquished the use of all German titles and styles, and adopted English surnames. George compensated several of his male relatives by creating them British peers. Thus, his cousin, <a href="/wiki/Louis_Mountbatten,_1st_Marquess_of_Milford_Haven" class="mw-redirect" title="Louis Mountbatten, 1st Marquess of Milford Haven">Prince Louis of Battenberg</a>, became the Marquess of Milford Haven, while his brother-in-law, the <a href="/wiki/Adolphus_Cambridge,_1st_Marquess_of_Cambridge" title="Adolphus Cambridge, 1st Marquess of Cambridge">Duke of Teck</a>, became the Marquess of Cambridge. Others, such as <a href="/wiki/Princess_Marie_Louise_of_Schleswig-Holstein" title="Princess Marie Louise of Schleswig-Holstein">Princess Marie Louise of Schleswig-Holstein</a> and <a href="/wiki/Princess_Helena_Victoria_of_Schleswig-Holstein" title="Princess Helena Victoria of Schleswig-Holstein">Princess Helena Victoria of Schleswig-Holstein</a>, simply stopped using their territorial designations. The system for titling members of the royal family was also simplified.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> Relatives of the British royal family who fought on the German side were simply cut off; their British peerages were suspended by a 1919 Order in Council under the provisions of the <a href="/wiki/Titles_Deprivation_Act_1917" title="Titles Deprivation Act 1917">Titles Deprivation Act 1917</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Tsar_Nicholas_II_%26_King_George_V.JPG" class="image"><img alt="Two look-alike men. Both wear beards and are in full military regalia festooned with medals\xe2\x80\x94one uniform dark, the other white." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/8/84/Tsar_Nicholas_II_%26_King_George_V.JPG/220px-Tsar_Nicholas_II_%26_King_George_V.JPG" width="220" height="311" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/8/84/Tsar_Nicholas_II_%26_King_George_V.JPG/330px-Tsar_Nicholas_II_%26_King_George_V.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/8/84/Tsar_Nicholas_II_%26_King_George_V.JPG/440px-Tsar_Nicholas_II_%26_King_George_V.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2128" data-file-height="3009" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Tsar_Nicholas_II_%26_King_George_V.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>King George V (right) with his first cousin <a href="/wiki/Nicholas_II_of_Russia" title="Nicholas II of Russia">Tsar Nicholas&#160;II</a>, Berlin, 1913</div></div></div>\n<p>Developments in Russia posed another set of issues for the monarchy. <a href="/wiki/Nicholas_II_of_Russia" title="Nicholas II of Russia">Tsar Nicholas&#160;II of Russia</a> was King George\'s first cousin and the two monarchs looked very much alike.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> When Nicholas was overthrown in the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Revolution_of_1917" class="mw-redirect" title="Russian Revolution of 1917">Russian Revolution of 1917</a>, the liberal Russian Government asked that the tsar and his family be given asylum in Britain.  The cabinet agreed but the king was worried that public opinion was hostile and said no.  It is likely the tsar would have refused to leave Russia in any case. He remained and in 1918 he and his family were ordered killed by Lenin, the Bolshevik leader.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Prince_of_Wales" title="Prince of Wales">Prince of Wales</a> \xe2\x80\x93 the future <a href="/wiki/Edward_VIII" title="Edward VIII">Edward VIII</a> \xe2\x80\x93 was keen to participate in the war but the government refused to allow it, citing the immense harm that would occur if the heir to the throne were captured.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> Despite this, Edward witnessed trench warfare at first hand and attempted to visit the front line as often as he could, for which he was awarded the <a href="/wiki/Military_Cross" title="Military Cross">Military Cross</a> in 1916. His role in the war, although limited, led to his great popularity among veterans of the conflict.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Beatty_Rodman_KingGeorgeV_KingEdwardVIII_Sims.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Five men, all in immaculate naval dress, stand on deck." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Beatty_Rodman_KingGeorgeV_KingEdwardVIII_Sims.jpg/220px-Beatty_Rodman_KingGeorgeV_KingEdwardVIII_Sims.jpg" width="220" height="160" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Beatty_Rodman_KingGeorgeV_KingEdwardVIII_Sims.jpg/330px-Beatty_Rodman_KingGeorgeV_KingEdwardVIII_Sims.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Beatty_Rodman_KingGeorgeV_KingEdwardVIII_Sims.jpg/440px-Beatty_Rodman_KingGeorgeV_KingEdwardVIII_Sims.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1719" data-file-height="1248" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Beatty_Rodman_KingGeorgeV_KingEdwardVIII_Sims.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/George_V" title="George V">King George V</a> and his son the <a href="/wiki/Edward_VIII" title="Edward VIII">Prince of Wales</a> visiting the Grand Fleet in 1918. From left to right: Admiral <a href="/wiki/David_Beatty,_1st_Earl_Beatty" title="David Beatty, 1st Earl Beatty">David Beatty</a>, <a href="/wiki/Royal_Navy" title="Royal Navy">RN</a>; Rear Admiral <a href="/wiki/Hugh_Rodman" title="Hugh Rodman">Hugh Rodman</a>, <a href="/wiki/USN" class="mw-redirect" title="USN">USN</a>; King George V; Prince of Wales; Vice Admiral <a href="/wiki/William_S._Sims" class="mw-redirect" title="William S. Sims">William S. Sims</a>, USN</div></div></div>\n<p>Other members of the royal family were similarly involved. Prince Albert, <a href="/wiki/Duke_of_York" title="Duke of York">Duke of York</a> (later <a href="/wiki/George_VI" title="George VI">George VI</a>), was commissioned in the Royal Navy and saw action as a turret officer aboard <a href="/wiki/HMS_Collingwood_(1908)" title="HMS Collingwood (1908)">HMS <i>Collingwood</i></a> at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Jutland" title="Battle of Jutland">battle of Jutland</a> but saw no further action in the war, largely because of ill health.<sup id="cite_ref-Bradford55_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bradford55-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Mary,_Princess_Royal_and_Countess_of_Harewood" title="Mary, Princess Royal and Countess of Harewood">Princess Mary</a>, the King\'s only daughter, visited hospitals and welfare organisations with her mother, assisting with projects to give comfort to British servicemen and assistance to their families. One of these projects was <a href="/wiki/Princess_Mary_Christmas_gift_box" title="Princess Mary Christmas gift box">Princess Mary\'s <i>Christmas Gift Fund</i></a>, through which \xc2\xa3162,000 worth of gifts was sent to all British soldiers and sailors for Christmas 1914.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> She took an active role in promoting the <a href="/wiki/Girlguiding_UK" class="mw-redirect" title="Girlguiding UK">Girl Guide</a> movement, the <a href="/wiki/Voluntary_Aid_Detachment" title="Voluntary Aid Detachment">Voluntary Aid Detachment</a> (VAD), the <a href="/wiki/Women%27s_Land_Army" title="Women&#39;s Land Army">Land Girls</a> and in 1918, she took a nursing course and went to work at <a href="/wiki/Great_Ormond_Street_Hospital" title="Great Ormond Street Hospital">Great Ormond Street Hospital</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Defence_of_the_Realm_Act">Defence of the Realm Act</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_the_United_Kingdom_during_the_First_World_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Defence of the Realm Act">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Defence_of_the_Realm_Act_1914" title="Defence of the Realm Act 1914">Defence of the Realm Act 1914</a></div>\n<p>The first Defence of the Realm Act (DORA) was passed on <span class="nowrap">8 August</span> 1914, during the early weeks of the war,<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> though in the next few months its provisions were extended.<sup id="cite_ref-beck348_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-beck348-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> It gave the <a href="/wiki/British_government" class="mw-redirect" title="British government">government</a> wide-ranging powers,<sup id="cite_ref-beck348_65-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-beck348-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> such as the ability to <a href="/wiki/Eminent_domain" title="Eminent domain">requisition</a> buildings or land needed for the <a href="/wiki/War_effort" title="War effort">war effort</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> Some of the things the British public were prohibited from doing included loitering under railway bridges,<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> feeding wild animals<sup id="cite_ref-beck381_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-beck381-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> and discussing naval and military matters.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/British_Summer_Time" title="British Summer Time">British Summer Time</a> was also introduced.<sup id="cite_ref-beck383_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-beck383-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> Alcoholic beverages were now to be watered down, pub closing times were brought forward from <span class="nowrap">12.30 am</span> to <span class="nowrap">10 pm</span>, and, from <span class="nowrap">August 1916</span>, Londoners were no longer able to whistle for a <a href="/wiki/Cabriolet_(carriage)" title="Cabriolet (carriage)">cab</a> between <span class="nowrap">10 pm</span> and <span class="nowrap">7 am</span>.<sup id="cite_ref-beck383_70-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-beck383-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> It has been criticised for both its strength and its use of the <a href="/wiki/Death_penalty" class="mw-redirect" title="Death penalty">death penalty</a> as a <a href="/wiki/Deterrence_(legal)" title="Deterrence (legal)">deterrent</a><sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup>&#160;\xe2\x80\x93 although the act itself did not refer to the death penalty, it made provision for civilians breaking these rules to be tried in army <a href="/wiki/Court_martial" class="mw-redirect" title="Court martial">courts martial</a>, where the maximum penalty was death.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Internment">Internment</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_the_United_Kingdom_during_the_First_World_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Internment">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Internment_in_the_United_Kingdom_during_the_First_World_War" title="Internment in the United Kingdom during the First World War">Internment in the United Kingdom during the First World War</a></div>\n<p>The Aliens Restriction Act, passed on 5 August, required all foreign nationals to register with the police, and by 9 September just under 67,000 German, Austrian and Hungarian nationals had done so.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> Citizens of enemy states residing in the UK were subject to restrictions on travel, possession of equipment that might be used for espionage, and residence in areas likely to be invaded.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> The government was reluctant to impose widespread <a href="/wiki/Internment_in_the_United_Kingdom_during_the_First_World_War" title="Internment in the United Kingdom during the First World War">internment</a>. It rescinded a military decision of 7 August 1914 to intern all nationals of enemy states between the ages of 17 and 42, and focussed instead only on those suspected of being a threat to national security. By September, 10,500 aliens were being held, but between November 1914 and April 1915 few arrests were made and thousands of internees were actually released. Public anti-German sentiment, which had been building since October following reports of <a href="/wiki/Rape_of_Belgium" title="Rape of Belgium">German atrocities in Belgium</a>, peaked after the <a href="/wiki/Sinking_of_the_RMS_Lusitania" title="Sinking of the RMS Lusitania">sinking of the RMS Lusitania</a> on 7 May 1915. The incident prompted a week of rioting across the UK, during which virtually every German-owned shop had its windows broken. The reaction forced the government to implement a tougher policy on internment, as much for the aliens own safety as for the security of the country. All non-<a href="/wiki/Naturalization" title="Naturalization">naturalised</a> enemy nationals of military age were to be interned, while those over military age were to be repatriated, and by 1917 only a small number of enemy nationals were still residing at liberty.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Armed_forces">Armed forces</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_the_United_Kingdom_during_the_First_World_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Armed forces">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Army">Army</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_the_United_Kingdom_during_the_First_World_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Army">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:British_recruits_August_1914_Q53234.jpg" class="image"><img alt="A large throng of people congregate, surrounded by police officers. Approximately half wear peaked caps; the rest wear boaters. Some smile, the rest look pensive." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/British_recruits_August_1914_Q53234.jpg/220px-British_recruits_August_1914_Q53234.jpg" width="220" height="152" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/British_recruits_August_1914_Q53234.jpg/330px-British_recruits_August_1914_Q53234.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/British_recruits_August_1914_Q53234.jpg/440px-British_recruits_August_1914_Q53234.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1247" data-file-height="859" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:British_recruits_August_1914_Q53234.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>August 1914: London army volunteers await their pay at <a href="/wiki/St._Martin-in-the-Fields" class="mw-redirect" title="St. Martin-in-the-Fields">St. Martin-in-the-Fields</a></div></div></div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/British_Army_during_World_War_I" title="British Army during World War I">British Army during World War&#160;I</a> was small in size when compared to the other major European powers. In 1914, the British had a small, largely urban English, volunteer force<sup id="cite_ref-beck289_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-beck289-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> of 400,000 soldiers, almost half of whom were posted overseas to garrison the British Empire. (In <span class="nowrap">August 1914</span>, 74 of the 157 infantry battalions and 12 of the 31 cavalry regiments were posted overseas.<sup id="cite_ref-TuckerRobertsEnc_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TuckerRobertsEnc-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>504</span></sup>) This total included the Regular Army and reservists in the <a href="/wiki/Territorial_Force" title="Territorial Force">Territorial Force</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-TuckerRobertsEnc_5-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TuckerRobertsEnc-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>504</span></sup> Together they formed the <a href="/wiki/British_Expeditionary_Force_(World_War_I)" title="British Expeditionary Force (World War I)">British Expeditionary Force</a> (BEF),<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> for service in France and became known as the <a href="/wiki/Old_Contemptibles" class="mw-redirect" title="Old Contemptibles">Old Contemptibles</a>. The mass of volunteers in 1914\xe2\x80\x931915, popularly known as <a href="/wiki/Kitchener%27s_Army" title="Kitchener&#39;s Army">Kitchener\'s Army</a>, was destined to go into action at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Somme" title="Battle of the Somme">battle of the Somme</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-TuckerRobertsEnc_5-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TuckerRobertsEnc-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>504</span></sup> In <span class="nowrap">January 1916</span>, conscription was introduced, and by the end of 1918, the army had reached its peak of strength of four million men.<sup id="cite_ref-TuckerRobertsEnc_5-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TuckerRobertsEnc-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>504</span></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Royal_Navy">Royal Navy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_the_United_Kingdom_during_the_First_World_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Royal Navy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Naval_warfare_of_World_War_I" title="Naval warfare of World War I">Naval warfare of World War&#160;I</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:2nd_Battle_Squadron.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Four battleships at sea" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/2nd_Battle_Squadron.jpg/220px-2nd_Battle_Squadron.jpg" width="220" height="126" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/2nd_Battle_Squadron.jpg/330px-2nd_Battle_Squadron.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/2nd_Battle_Squadron.jpg/440px-2nd_Battle_Squadron.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1200" data-file-height="685" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:2nd_Battle_Squadron.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ships of the 2nd Battle Squadron of the Royal Navy\'s Grand Fleet</div></div></div>\n<p>The Royal Navy at the start of the war was the largest navy in the world due, for the most part, to the <a href="/wiki/Naval_Defence_Act_1889" title="Naval Defence Act 1889">Naval Defence Act 1889</a> and the <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Royal_Navy#Two-power_standard&quot;" title="History of the Royal Navy">two-power standard</a> which called for the navy to maintain a number of battleships such as their strength was at least equal to the combined strength of the next two largest navies in the world, which at that point were <a href="/wiki/French_navy#19th_century_revival" class="mw-redirect" title="French navy">France</a> and <a href="/wiki/Russian_Navy#Imperial_Russian_Navy" title="Russian Navy">Russia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The major part of the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Navy" title="Royal Navy">Royal Navy</a>\'s strength was deployed at home in the <a href="/wiki/British_Grand_Fleet" class="mw-redirect" title="British Grand Fleet">Grand Fleet</a>, with the primary aim of drawing the <a href="/wiki/Hochseeflotte" class="mw-redirect" title="Hochseeflotte">German High Seas Fleet</a> into an engagement. No decisive victory ever came. The Royal Navy and the <a href="/wiki/Kaiserliche_Marine" class="mw-redirect" title="Kaiserliche Marine">German Imperial Navy</a> did come into contact, notably in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Heligoland_(1914)" class="mw-redirect" title="Battle of Heligoland (1914)">Battle of Heligoland Bight</a>, and at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Jutland" title="Battle of Jutland">Battle of Jutland</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-rn1914_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rn1914-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> In view of their inferior numbers and firepower, the Germans devised a plan to draw part of the British fleet into a trap and put it into effect at Jutland in <span class="nowrap">May 1916</span>, but the result was inconclusive. In <span class="nowrap">August 1916</span>, the High Seas Fleet tried a similar enticement operation and was "lucky to escape annihilation".<sup id="cite_ref-rn1916_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rn1916-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> The lessons learned by the Royal Navy at Jutland made it a more effective force in the future.<sup id="cite_ref-rn1916_83-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rn1916-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1914, the navy had also formed the <a href="/wiki/63rd_(Royal_Naval)_Division" title="63rd (Royal Naval) Division">63rd (Royal Naval) Division</a> from reservists, and this served extensively in the Mediterranean and on the <a href="/wiki/Western_Front_(World_War_I)" title="Western Front (World War I)">Western Front</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-rn1914_82-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rn1914-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> Almost half of the Royal Navy casualties during the War were sustained by this division, fighting on land and not at sea.<sup id="cite_ref-rn1914_82-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rn1914-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="British_air_services">British air services</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_the_United_Kingdom_during_the_First_World_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: British air services">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Royal_Flying_Corps" title="Royal Flying Corps">Royal Flying Corps</a> and <a href="/wiki/Royal_Naval_Air_Service" title="Royal Naval Air Service">Royal Naval Air Service</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Royal_Flying_Corps_poster.jpg" class="image"><img alt="A poster reads &quot;ROYAL FLYING CORPS&quot;, &quot;MILITARY WING&quot;, &quot;VACANCIES EXIST&quot;, information about pay, &quot;GOD SAVE THE KING&quot;." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Royal_Flying_Corps_poster.jpg/220px-Royal_Flying_Corps_poster.jpg" width="220" height="268" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Royal_Flying_Corps_poster.jpg/330px-Royal_Flying_Corps_poster.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Royal_Flying_Corps_poster.jpg/440px-Royal_Flying_Corps_poster.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1368" data-file-height="1669" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Royal_Flying_Corps_poster.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Royal_Flying_Corps" title="Royal Flying Corps">Royal Flying Corps</a> recruitment poster</div></div></div>\n<p>At the start of the war, the Royal Flying Corps (RFC), commanded by <a href="/wiki/David_Henderson_(British_Army_officer)" title="David Henderson (British Army officer)">David Henderson</a>, was sent to France and was first used for aerial spotting in <span class="nowrap">September 1914</span>, but only became efficient when they perfected the use of <a href="/wiki/Wireless" title="Wireless">wireless communication</a> at <a href="/wiki/Aubers_Ridge" class="mw-redirect" title="Aubers Ridge">Aubers Ridge</a> on <span class="nowrap">9 May</span> 1915. <a href="/wiki/Aerial_photography" title="Aerial photography">Aerial photography</a> was attempted during 1914, but again only became effective the next year. In 1915 <a href="/wiki/Hugh_Trenchard" class="mw-redirect" title="Hugh Trenchard">Hugh Trenchard</a> replaced Henderson and the RFC adopted an aggressive posture. By 1918, photographic images could be taken from 15,000 feet (4,600&#160;m), and interpreted by over 3,000 personnel. Planes did not carry <a href="/wiki/Parachute" title="Parachute">parachutes</a> until 1918, though they had been available since before the war.<sup id="cite_ref-beckett254_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-beckett254-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup>\nOn 17 August 1917, General <a href="/wiki/Jan_Smuts" title="Jan Smuts">Jan Smuts</a> presented a report to the War Council on the future of <a href="/wiki/Aerial_warfare" title="Aerial warfare">air power</a>. Because of its potential for the \'devastation of enemy lands and the destruction of industrial and populous centres on a vast scale\', he recommended a new air service be formed that would be on a level with the army and navy. The formation of the new service however would make the under utilised men and machines of the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Naval_Air_Service" title="Royal Naval Air Service">Royal Naval Air Service</a> (RNAS) available for action across <a href="/wiki/Western_Front_(World_War_I)" title="Western Front (World War I)">the Western Front</a>, as well as ending the inter-service rivalries that at times had adversely affected aircraft procurement. On <span class="nowrap">1 April</span> 1918, the RFC and the RNAS were amalgamated to form a new service, the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Air_Force" title="Royal Air Force">Royal Air Force</a> (RAF).<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Recruitment_and_conscription">Recruitment and conscription</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_the_United_Kingdom_during_the_First_World_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Recruitment and conscription">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Recruitment_to_the_British_Army_during_the_First_World_War" title="Recruitment to the British Army during the First World War">Recruitment to the British Army during the First World War</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Britain_Needs_You_at_Once_-_WWI_recruitment_poster_-_Parliamentary_Recruiting_Committee_Poster_No._108.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Britain_Needs_You_at_Once_-_WWI_recruitment_poster_-_Parliamentary_Recruiting_Committee_Poster_No._108.jpg/200px-Britain_Needs_You_at_Once_-_WWI_recruitment_poster_-_Parliamentary_Recruiting_Committee_Poster_No._108.jpg" width="200" height="304" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Britain_Needs_You_at_Once_-_WWI_recruitment_poster_-_Parliamentary_Recruiting_Committee_Poster_No._108.jpg/300px-Britain_Needs_You_at_Once_-_WWI_recruitment_poster_-_Parliamentary_Recruiting_Committee_Poster_No._108.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Britain_Needs_You_at_Once_-_WWI_recruitment_poster_-_Parliamentary_Recruiting_Committee_Poster_No._108.jpg/400px-Britain_Needs_You_at_Once_-_WWI_recruitment_poster_-_Parliamentary_Recruiting_Committee_Poster_No._108.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2534" data-file-height="3852" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Britain_Needs_You_at_Once_-_WWI_recruitment_poster_-_Parliamentary_Recruiting_Committee_Poster_No._108.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Poster by the Parliamentary Recruiting Committee, featuring <a href="/wiki/St._George_and_the_Dragon" class="mw-redirect" title="St. George and the Dragon">St. George and the Dragon</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>Particularly in the early stages of the war, many men, for a wide variety of reasons, decided to "join up" to the armed forces\xe2\x80\x94by <span class="nowrap">5 September</span> 1914, over 225,000 had signed up to fight for what became known as <a href="/wiki/Kitchener%27s_Army" title="Kitchener&#39;s Army">Kitchener\'s Army</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-beck291_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-beck291-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> Over the course of the war, a number of factors contributed to recruitment rates, including patriotism, the work of the Parliamentary Recruiting Committee in producing posters, dwindling alternative employment opportunities, and an eagerness for adventure to escape humdrum routine.<sup id="cite_ref-beck291_86-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-beck291-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Pals_battalions" class="mw-redirect" title="Pals battalions">Pals battalions</a>, where whole battalions were raised from a small geographic area or employer, also proved popular. Higher recruitment rates were seen in Wales and Scotland, though in the case of the Welsh and Irish, political tensions tended to "put something of a blight upon enlistment".<sup id="cite_ref-beck291_86-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-beck291-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Lawson_Wood_-_Parliamentary_Recruiting_Committee_-_Your_King_%26_Country_Need_You.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/94/Lawson_Wood_-_Parliamentary_Recruiting_Committee_-_Your_King_%26_Country_Need_You.jpg/220px-Lawson_Wood_-_Parliamentary_Recruiting_Committee_-_Your_King_%26_Country_Need_You.jpg" width="220" height="328" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/94/Lawson_Wood_-_Parliamentary_Recruiting_Committee_-_Your_King_%26_Country_Need_You.jpg/330px-Lawson_Wood_-_Parliamentary_Recruiting_Committee_-_Your_King_%26_Country_Need_You.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/94/Lawson_Wood_-_Parliamentary_Recruiting_Committee_-_Your_King_%26_Country_Need_You.jpg/440px-Lawson_Wood_-_Parliamentary_Recruiting_Committee_-_Your_King_%26_Country_Need_You.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4449" data-file-height="6631" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Lawson_Wood_-_Parliamentary_Recruiting_Committee_-_Your_King_%26_Country_Need_You.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A 1914 poster shows a Scottish soldier in Belgium, in response to Germany describing the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_London_(1839)" title="Treaty of London (1839)">Treaty of London</a>, which protected Belgium\'s independence and neutrality, as a "scrap of paper" when they invaded in August 1914.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>Recruitment remained fairly steady through 1914 and early 1915, but fell dramatically during the later years, especially after the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Somme" title="Battle of the Somme">Somme campaign</a>, which resulted in 500,000 casualties. As a result, <a href="/wiki/Conscription_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Conscription in the United Kingdom">conscription</a> was introduced for the first time in <span class="nowrap">January 1916</span> for single men, and extended in May\xe2\x80\x93June to all men aged 18 to 41 across England, Wales and Scotland, by way of the <i><a href="/wiki/Military_Service_Act_(United_Kingdom)" class="mw-redirect" title="Military Service Act (United Kingdom)">Military Service Acts</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-beck291_86-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-beck291-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-bbc_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbc-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Urban centres, with their poverty and unemployment were favourite recruiting grounds of the regular British army. Dundee, where the female dominated jute industry limited male employment had one of the highest proportion of reservists and serving soldiers than almost any other British city.<sup id="cite_ref-Lenman&amp;Mackie1991_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lenman&amp;Mackie1991-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> Concern for their families\' standard of living made men hesitate to enlist; voluntary enlistment rates went up after the government guaranteed a weekly stipend for life to the survivors of men who were killed or disabled.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> After the introduction of conscription from January 1916 every part of the country was affected. \n</p><p>The policy of relying on volunteers had sharply reduced the capacity of heavy industry to produce the munitions needed for the war.  Historian <a href="/wiki/R._J._Q._Adams" title="R. J. Q. Adams">R. J. Q. Adams</a> reports that 19% of the men in the iron and steel industry entered the Army, 22% of the miners, 20% in the engineering trades, 24% in the electrical industries, 16% among small arms craftsmen, and 24% of the men who had been engaged in making high explosives.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> In response critical industries were prioritised over the army ("reserved occupations"), including munitions, food production and merchant shipping.<sup id="cite_ref-beck291_86-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-beck291-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Conscription_Crisis_of_1918">Conscription Crisis of 1918</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_the_United_Kingdom_during_the_First_World_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Conscription Crisis of 1918">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Conscription_Crisis_of_1918" title="Conscription Crisis of 1918">Conscription Crisis of 1918</a></div>\n<p>In April 1918 legislation was brought forward which allowed for extension of conscription to Ireland.<sup id="cite_ref-beck291_86-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-beck291-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> Though this ultimately never materialised, the effect was "disastrous".<sup id="cite_ref-beck291_86-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-beck291-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> Despite significant numbers <a href="/wiki/Ireland_in_World_War_I" class="mw-redirect" title="Ireland in World War I">volunteering for Irish regiments</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-beck291_86-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-beck291-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> the idea of <i>enforced</i> conscription proved unpopular. The reaction was based particularly on the fact that implementation of conscription in Ireland was linked to a pledged "measure of self-government in Ireland".<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> The linking of conscription and <a href="/wiki/Government_of_Ireland_Act_1914" title="Government of Ireland Act 1914">Home Rule</a> in this way outraged the Irish parties at Westminster, who walked out in protest and returned to Ireland to organise opposition.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup>  As a result, a general strike was called, and on <span class="nowrap">23 April</span> 1918, work was stopped in railways, docks, factories, mills, theatres, cinemas, trams, public services, shipyards, newspapers, shops, and even official munitions factories.  The strike was described as "complete and entire, an unprecedented event outside the <a href="/wiki/Continental_Europe" title="Continental Europe">continental countries</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> Ultimately the effect was a total loss of interest in Home Rule and of popular support for the nationalist <a href="/wiki/Irish_Parliamentary_Party" title="Irish Parliamentary Party">Irish Party</a> who were defeated outright by the separatist republican <a href="/wiki/Sinn_F%C3%A9in" title="Sinn F\xc3\xa9in">Sinn F\xc3\xa9in</a> party in the December 1918 <a href="/wiki/Irish_general_election,_1918" title="Irish general election, 1918">Irish general election</a>, one of the precursors of the <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Irish_War" class="mw-redirect" title="Anglo-Irish War">Anglo-Irish War</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Conscientious_objectors">Conscientious objectors</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_the_United_Kingdom_during_the_First_World_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit
