section: Conscientious objectors">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Conscientious_objector#United_Kingdom" title="Conscientious objector">Conscientious objector \xc2\xa7&#160;United Kingdom</a></div>\n<p>The conscription legislation introduced the right to refuse military service, allowing for conscientious objectors to be absolutely exempted, to perform alternative <a href="/wiki/Civilian_service" class="mw-redirect" title="Civilian service">civilian service</a>, or to serve as a <a href="/wiki/Non-combatant" title="Non-combatant">non-combatant</a> in the army, according to the extent to which they could convince a Military Service Tribunal of the quality of their objection.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> Around 16,500 men were recorded as conscientious objectors,<sup id="cite_ref-beck291_86-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-beck291-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> with <a href="/wiki/Quakers" title="Quakers">Quakers</a> playing a large role.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2009)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> 4,500 objectors were sent to work on farms to undertake "work of national importance", 7,000 were ordered non-combatant duties as stretcher bearers, but 6,000 were forced into the army, and when they refused orders, they were sent to prison, as in the case of the <a href="/wiki/Richmond_Sixteen" title="Richmond Sixteen">Richmond Sixteen</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup>\nSome 843 conscientious objectors spent more than two years in prison; ten died while there, seventeen were initially given the death penalty (but received life imprisonment) and 142 were imprisoned on life sentences.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> Conscientious objectors who were deemed not to have made any useful contribution were <a href="/wiki/Disfranchisement" title="Disfranchisement">disenfranchised</a> for five years after the war.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_95-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Naval_and_air_raids">Naval and air raids</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_the_United_Kingdom_during_the_First_World_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Naval and air raids">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:WWI_German_targets_in_England_(zoomed).png" class="image"><img alt="A map of England, with towns bombarded during the war marked. All are in the east." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/WWI_German_targets_in_England_%28zoomed%29.png/220px-WWI_German_targets_in_England_%28zoomed%29.png" width="220" height="213" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/5d/WWI_German_targets_in_England_%28zoomed%29.png 1.5x" data-file-width="279" data-file-height="270" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:WWI_German_targets_in_England_(zoomed).png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>German bombardments were concentrated on the east coast of England</div></div></div>\n<p>At the start of the First World War, for the first time since the <a href="/wiki/Napoleonic_Wars" title="Napoleonic Wars">Napoleonic Wars</a>, the population of the British Isles was in danger of attack from naval raids. The country also came under attack from air raids by <a href="/wiki/Zeppelins" class="mw-redirect" title="Zeppelins">zeppelins</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fixed-wing_aircraft" title="Fixed-wing aircraft">fixed-wing aircraft</a>, another first.<sup id="cite_ref-TuckerRobertsEnc_5-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TuckerRobertsEnc-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>709</span></sup><sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Naval_raids">Naval raids</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_the_United_Kingdom_during_the_First_World_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Naval raids">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Raid_on_Yarmouth" title="Raid on Yarmouth">Raid on Yarmouth</a> and <a href="/wiki/Raid_on_Scarborough,_Hartlepool_and_Whitby" title="Raid on Scarborough, Hartlepool and Whitby">Raid on Scarborough, Hartlepool and Whitby</a></div>\n<p>The Raid on Yarmouth, which took place in <span class="nowrap">November 1914</span>, was an attack by the German Navy on the British <a href="/wiki/North_Sea" title="North Sea">North Sea</a> port and town of <a href="/wiki/Great_Yarmouth" title="Great Yarmouth">Great Yarmouth</a>. Little damage was done to the town itself, since shells only landed on the beach once German ships laying <a href="/wiki/Naval_mine" title="Naval mine">mines</a> offshore were interrupted by British destroyers. One British <a href="/wiki/Submarine" title="Submarine">submarine</a> was sunk by a mine as it attempted to leave harbour and attack the German ships, while one German <a href="/wiki/Armored_cruiser" title="Armored cruiser">armoured cruiser</a> was sunk after striking two mines outside its own home port.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Rememberscarborough.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Britannia stands in front of a group of men holding various armaments, looking out over a scene of burning houses. The caption reads &quot;REMEMBER SCARBOROUGH!&quot;, &quot;ENLIST NOW&quot;." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/8/89/Rememberscarborough.jpg/220px-Rememberscarborough.jpg" width="220" height="332" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/8/89/Rememberscarborough.jpg/330px-Rememberscarborough.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/8/89/Rememberscarborough.jpg 2x" data-file-width="386" data-file-height="582" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Rememberscarborough.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>British propaganda fuelled by the German raid on Scarborough</div></div></div>\n<p>In December 1914, the German navy carried out attacks on the British coastal towns of <a href="/wiki/Scarborough,_North_Yorkshire" title="Scarborough, North Yorkshire">Scarborough</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hartlepool" title="Hartlepool">Hartlepool</a> and <a href="/wiki/Whitby" title="Whitby">Whitby</a>. The attack resulted in 137 fatalities and 593 casualties,<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> many of which were civilians. The attack made the German navy very unpopular with the British public, as an attack against British <a href="/wiki/Civilian" title="Civilian">civilians</a> in their homes. Likewise, the British Royal Navy was criticised for failing to prevent the raid.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bombardment_of_Yarmouth_and_Lowestoft">Bombardment of Yarmouth and Lowestoft</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_the_United_Kingdom_during_the_First_World_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Bombardment of Yarmouth and Lowestoft">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Bombardment_of_Yarmouth_and_Lowestoft" title="Bombardment of Yarmouth and Lowestoft">Bombardment of Yarmouth and Lowestoft</a></div>\n<p>In April 1916 a German battlecruiser squadron with accompanying cruisers and destroyers bombarded the coastal ports of Yarmouth and Lowestoft. Although the ports had some military importance, the main aim of the raid was to entice out defending ships which could then be picked off either by the battlecruiser squadron or by the full High Seas Fleet, which was stationed at sea ready to intervene if an opportunity presented itself. The result was inconclusive: nearby Royal Navy units were too small to intervene so largely kept clear of the German battlecruisers, and the German ships withdrew before the first British fast response battlecruiser squadron or the Grand Fleet could arrive.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:It_is_far_better_to_face_the_bullets.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Poster: picture of Zeppelin illuminated by searchlight over silhouetted London skyline; headline: &quot;IT IS FAR BETTER TO FACE THE BULLETS THAN TO BE KILLED AT HOME BY A BOMB&quot;" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/It_is_far_better_to_face_the_bullets.jpg/220px-It_is_far_better_to_face_the_bullets.jpg" width="220" height="325" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/It_is_far_better_to_face_the_bullets.jpg/330px-It_is_far_better_to_face_the_bullets.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/It_is_far_better_to_face_the_bullets.jpg/440px-It_is_far_better_to_face_the_bullets.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2685" data-file-height="3968" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:It_is_far_better_to_face_the_bullets.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>British propaganda poster from 1915, drawing on the fear of zeppelin attacks to aid recruitment</div></div></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Air_raids">Air raids</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_the_United_Kingdom_during_the_First_World_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Air raids">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Zeppelin#WW1" title="Zeppelin">Zeppelins in World War&#160;I</a></div>\n<p>German <a href="/wiki/Zeppelins" class="mw-redirect" title="Zeppelins">zeppelins</a> bombed towns on the east coast, starting on <span class="nowrap">19 January</span> 1915 with <a href="/wiki/Great_Yarmouth" title="Great Yarmouth">Great Yarmouth</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-beckett258_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-beckett258-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> London was also hit later in the same year, on <span class="nowrap">31 May</span>.<sup id="cite_ref-beckett258_104-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-beckett258-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Propaganda" title="Propaganda">Propaganda</a> supporting the British war effort often used these raids to their advantage: one recruitment poster claimed: "It is far better to face the bullets than to be killed at home by a bomb" (see image). The reaction from the public, however, was mixed; whilst 10,000 visited <a href="/wiki/Scarborough,_North_Yorkshire" title="Scarborough, North Yorkshire">Scarborough</a> to view the damage there, London theatres reported having fewer visitors during periods of "Zeppelin weather"\xe2\x80\x94dark, fine nights.<sup id="cite_ref-beckett258_104-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-beckett258-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Throughout 1917 <a href="/wiki/Imperial_Germany" class="mw-redirect" title="Imperial Germany">Germany</a> began to deploy increasing numbers of <a href="/wiki/Fixed-wing_aircraft" title="Fixed-wing aircraft">fixed-wing</a> <a href="/wiki/Bombers" class="mw-redirect" title="Bombers">bombers</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Gotha_G.IV" title="Gotha G.IV">Gotha G.IV</a>\'s first target being <a href="/wiki/Folkestone" title="Folkestone">Folkestone</a> on <span class="nowrap">25 May</span> 1917, following this attack the number of airship raids decreased rapidly in favour of raids by fixed wing aircraft,<sup id="cite_ref-beckett258_104-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-beckett258-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> before Zeppelin raids were called off entirely. In total, Zeppelins dropped 6,000 bombs, resulting in 556 dead and 1,357 wounded.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> Soon after the raid on Folkestone, the bombers began raids on London: one daylight raid on <span class="nowrap">13 June</span> 1917 by 14 Gothas caused 162 deaths in the <a href="/wiki/East_End_of_London" title="East End of London">East End of London</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-beckett258_104-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-beckett258-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> In response to this new threat, <a href="/wiki/Edward_Ashmore_(British_Army_officer)" title="Edward Ashmore (British Army officer)">Major General Edward Bailey Ashmore</a>, a RFC pilot who later commanded an <a href="/wiki/Artillery" title="Artillery">artillery</a> <a href="/wiki/Division_(military)" title="Division (military)">division</a> in Belgium, was appointed to devise an improved system of detection, communication and control,<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> The system, called the <i>Metropolitan Observation Service</i>, encompassed the <a href="/wiki/London_Air_Defence_Area" title="London Air Defence Area">London Air Defence Area</a> and would later extend eastwards towards the <a href="/wiki/Kent" title="Kent">Kentish</a> and <a href="/wiki/Essex" title="Essex">Essex</a> coasts. The Metropolitan Observation Service was fully operational until the late summer of 1918 (the last German bombing raid taking place on <span class="nowrap">19 May</span> 1918).<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup>\nDuring the war, the Germans carried out 51 airship raids and 52 fixed-wing bomber raids on England, which together dropped 280 tons of bombs. The casualties amounted to 1,413 killed, and 3,409 wounded.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> The success of anti-air defence measures was limited; of the 397 aircraft that had taken part in raids, only 24 Gothas were shot down (though 37 more were lost in accidents), despite an estimated rate of 14,540 anti-air rounds per aircraft. Anti-zeppelin defences were more successful, with 17 shot down and 21 lost in accidents.<sup id="cite_ref-beckett258_104-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-beckett258-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Media">Media</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_the_United_Kingdom_during_the_First_World_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Media">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Propaganda">Propaganda</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_the_United_Kingdom_during_the_First_World_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Propaganda">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/British_propaganda_during_World_War_I" title="British propaganda during World War I">British propaganda during World War&#160;I</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Stanford%E2%80%99s_Geographical_Establishment,_What_Germany_Wants_1917_Cornell_CUL_PJM_1199_01.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Stanford%E2%80%99s_Geographical_Establishment%2C_What_Germany_Wants_1917_Cornell_CUL_PJM_1199_01.jpg/220px-Stanford%E2%80%99s_Geographical_Establishment%2C_What_Germany_Wants_1917_Cornell_CUL_PJM_1199_01.jpg" width="220" height="149" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Stanford%E2%80%99s_Geographical_Establishment%2C_What_Germany_Wants_1917_Cornell_CUL_PJM_1199_01.jpg/330px-Stanford%E2%80%99s_Geographical_Establishment%2C_What_Germany_Wants_1917_Cornell_CUL_PJM_1199_01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Stanford%E2%80%99s_Geographical_Establishment%2C_What_Germany_Wants_1917_Cornell_CUL_PJM_1199_01.jpg/440px-Stanford%E2%80%99s_Geographical_Establishment%2C_What_Germany_Wants_1917_Cornell_CUL_PJM_1199_01.jpg 2x" data-file-width="6840" data-file-height="4632" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Stanford%E2%80%99s_Geographical_Establishment,_What_Germany_Wants_1917_Cornell_CUL_PJM_1199_01.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A world map showing territory  that "Germany Wants" by Edward Stanford. 1917. Close reading of the quoted material shows that the map is misleading: it implies that Germany plans to annex all the territory in red, but this is only the case for a small fraction of it.</div></div></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Propaganda" title="Propaganda">Propaganda</a> and censorship were closely linked during the war.<sup id="cite_ref-propaganda_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-propaganda-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> The need to maintain morale and counter German propaganda was recognised early in the war and the <a href="/wiki/War_Propaganda_Bureau" class="mw-redirect" title="War Propaganda Bureau">War Propaganda Bureau</a> was established under the leadership of <a href="/wiki/Charles_Masterman" title="Charles Masterman">Charles Masterman</a> in <span class="nowrap">September 1914</span>.<sup id="cite_ref-propaganda_109-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-propaganda-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> The Bureau enlisted eminent writers such as <a href="/wiki/H_G_Wells" class="mw-redirect" title="H G Wells">H G Wells</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Conan_Doyle" title="Arthur Conan Doyle">Arthur Conan Doyle</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rudyard_Kipling" title="Rudyard Kipling">Rudyard Kipling</a> as well as newspaper editors.  Until its abolition in 1917, the department published 300 books and pamphlets in 21 languages, distributed over 4,000 propaganda photographs every week, and circulated maps, cartoons, and lantern slides to the media.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup>  Masterman also commissioned films about the war such as <a href="/wiki/The_Battle_of_the_Somme_(film)" title="The Battle of the Somme (film)">The Battle of the Somme</a>, which appeared in <span class="nowrap">August 1916</span>, while the battle was still in progress as a morale-booster and in general it met with a favourable reception. <i>The Times</i> reported on <span class="nowrap">22 August</span> 1916 that "Crowded audiences&#160;... were interested and thrilled to have the realities of war brought so vividly before them, and if women had sometimes to shut their eyes to escape for a moment from the tragedy of the toll of battle which the film presents, opinion seems to be general that it was wise that the people at home should have this glimpse of what our soldiers are doing and daring and suffering in Picardy".<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Newspapers">Newspapers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_the_United_Kingdom_during_the_First_World_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Newspapers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Newspapers during the war were subject to the <i>Defence of the Realm Act</i>, which eventually had two regulations restricting what they could publish:<sup id="cite_ref-p22_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-p22-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> Regulation 18, which prohibited the leakage of sensitive military information, troop and shipping movements; and Regulation 27, which made it an offence to "spread false reports", "spread reports that were likely to prejudice recruiting", "undermine public confidence in banks or currency" or cause "disaffection to His Majesty".<sup id="cite_ref-p22_112-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-p22-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> Where the official <a href="/w/index.php?title=Press_Bureau&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Press Bureau (page does not exist)">Press Bureau</a> failed (it had no statutory powers until <span class="nowrap">April 1916</span>), the newspaper editors and owners operated a ruthless <a href="/wiki/Self-censorship" title="Self-censorship">self-censorship</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-beckett394_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-beckett394-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> Having worked for government, <a href="/wiki/Press_baron" class="mw-redirect" title="Press baron">press barons</a> <a href="/wiki/Harold_Harmsworth,_1st_Viscount_Rothermere" title="Harold Harmsworth, 1st Viscount Rothermere">Viscount Rothermere</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Max_Aitken,_1st_Baron_Beaverbrook" title="Max Aitken, 1st Baron Beaverbrook">Baron Beaverbrook</a> (in a sea of controversy),<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Harmsworth,_1st_Viscount_Northcliffe" title="Alfred Harmsworth, 1st Viscount Northcliffe">Viscount Northcliffe</a><sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> all received titles. For these reasons, it has been concluded that censorship, which at its height suppressed only socialist journals (and briefly the <a href="/wiki/Right-wing_politics" title="Right-wing politics">right wing</a> <i>The Globe</i>) had less effect on the British press than the reductions in advertising revenues and cost increases which they also faced during the war.<sup id="cite_ref-beckett394_10-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-beckett394-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> One major loophole in the official censorship lay with <a href="/wiki/Parliamentary_privilege" title="Parliamentary privilege">parliamentary privilege</a>, when anything said in Parliament could be reported freely.<sup id="cite_ref-p22_112-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-p22-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup>  The most infamous act of censorship in the early days of the war was the sinking of <a href="/wiki/HMS_Audacious_(1912)" title="HMS Audacious (1912)">HMS <i>Audacious</i></a> in <span class="nowrap">October 1914</span>, when the press was directed not to report on the loss, despite the sinking being observed by passengers on the liner <a href="/wiki/RMS_Olympic" title="RMS Olympic">RMS <i>Olympic</i></a> and quickly reported in the American press.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The most popular papers of the period included dailies such as <i><a href="/wiki/The_Times" title="The Times">The Times</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Daily_Telegraph" title="The Daily Telegraph">The Daily Telegraph</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Morning_Post" title="The Morning Post">The Morning Post</a></i>, weekly newspapers such as <i><a href="/wiki/The_Graphic" title="The Graphic">The Graphic</a></i> and periodicals like <i><a href="/wiki/John_Bull_(magazine)" title="John Bull (magazine)">John Bull</a></i>, which claimed a weekly circulation of 900,000.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup> The public demand for news of the war was reflected in the increased sales of newspapers. After the German Navy raid on Hartlepool and Scarborough, the <i><a href="/wiki/Daily_Mail" title="Daily Mail">Daily Mail</a></i> devoted three full pages to the raid and the <i><a href="/wiki/Evening_News_(London)" class="mw-redirect" title="Evening News (London)">Evening News</a></i> reported that <i>The Times</i> had sold out by a quarter past nine in the morning, even with inflated prices.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> The <i>Daily Mail</i> itself increased in circulation from 800,000 a day in 1914 to <span class="nowrap">1.5 million</span> by 1916.<sup id="cite_ref-beckett394_10-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-beckett394-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="News_magazines">News magazines</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_the_United_Kingdom_during_the_First_World_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: News magazines">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The public\'s thirst for news and information was in part satisfied by <a href="/wiki/Newsmagazine" class="mw-redirect" title="Newsmagazine">news magazines</a>, which were dedicated to reporting the war. They included amongst others <i><a href="/wiki/The_War_Illustrated" title="The War Illustrated">The War Illustrated</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Illustrated_War_News" title="The Illustrated War News">The Illustrated War News</a></i>, and <i><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_War_Pictorial&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="The War Pictorial (page does not exist)">The War Pictorial</a></i>, and were lavishly filled with photographs and illustrations, regardless of their <a href="/wiki/Target_audience" title="Target audience">target audience</a>. Magazines were produced for all classes, and ranged both in price and tone. Many otherwise famous writers contributed towards these publications, of which H.G. Wells, Arthur Conan Doyle and Rudyard Kipling were three examples. Editorial guidelines varied; in cheaper publications especially it was considered more important to create a sense of <a href="/wiki/Patriotism" title="Patriotism">patriotism</a> than to relay up-to-the-minutes news of developments of the front. Stories of German atrocities were commonplace.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Music">Music</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_the_United_Kingdom_during_the_First_World_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Music">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>On 13 August 1914, the Irish regiment the <a href="/wiki/Connaught_Rangers" title="Connaught Rangers">Connaught Rangers</a> were witnessed singing "<a href="/wiki/It%27s_a_Long_Way_to_Tipperary" title="It&#39;s a Long Way to Tipperary">It\'s a Long Way to Tipperary</a>" as they marched through <a href="/wiki/Boulogne-sur-Mer" title="Boulogne-sur-Mer">Boulogne</a> by the <i><a href="/wiki/Daily_Mail" title="Daily Mail">Daily Mail</a></i> correspondent George Curnock, who reported the event in that newspaper on <span class="nowrap">18 August</span> 1914. The song was then picked up by other units of the <a href="/wiki/British_Army" title="British Army">British Army</a>. In <span class="nowrap">November 1914</span>, it was sung in a <a href="/wiki/Pantomime" title="Pantomime">pantomime</a> by the well-known <a href="/wiki/Music_hall" title="Music hall">music hall</a> singer <a href="/wiki/Florrie_Forde" title="Florrie Forde">Florrie Forde</a>, which helped contribute to its worldwide popularity.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup>\nAnother song from 1916, which became very popular as a <a href="/wiki/Music_hall" title="Music hall">music hall</a> and <a href="/wiki/Marching_song" class="mw-redirect" title="Marching song">marching song</a>, boosting British morale despite the horrors of that war, was "<a href="/wiki/Pack_Up_Your_Troubles_in_Your_Old_Kit-Bag" title="Pack Up Your Troubles in Your Old Kit-Bag">Pack Up Your Troubles in Your Old Kit-Bag</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="War_poems">War poems</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_the_United_Kingdom_during_the_First_World_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: War poems">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/War_poet#WWI" title="War poet">War poetry during World War&#160;I</a></div>\n<p>There was also a notable group of <a href="/wiki/War_poet" title="War poet">war poets</a> who wrote about their own experiences of war, which caught the public attention. Some died on active service, most famously <a href="/wiki/Rupert_Brooke" title="Rupert Brooke">Rupert Brooke</a>, <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Rosenberg" title="Isaac Rosenberg">Isaac Rosenberg</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Wilfred_Owen" title="Wilfred Owen">Wilfred Owen</a>, while some, such as <a href="/wiki/Siegfried_Sassoon" title="Siegfried Sassoon">Siegfried Sassoon</a> survived. Themes of the poems included the youth (or naivety) of the soldiers, and the dignified manner in which they fought and died.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2009)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> This is evident in lines such as "They fell with their faces to the foe", from the "<a href="/wiki/Ode_of_Remembrance" title="Ode of Remembrance">Ode of Remembrance</a>" taken from <a href="/wiki/Laurence_Binyon" title="Laurence Binyon">Laurence Binyon</a>\'s <i><a href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/For_the_Fallen" class="extiw" title="s:For the Fallen">For the Fallen</a></i>, which was first published in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Times" title="The Times">The Times</a></i> in <span class="nowrap">September 1914</span>.<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup> Female poets such as <a href="/wiki/Vera_Brittain" title="Vera Brittain">Vera Brittain</a> also wrote from the <a href="/wiki/Home_front" title="Home front">home front</a>, to lament the losses of brothers and lovers fighting on the front.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Economy">Economy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_the_United_Kingdom_during_the_First_World_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Economy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Economic_history_of_World_War_I#British_Empire" title="Economic history of World War I">Economic history of World War I \xc2\xa7&#160;British Empire</a></div>\n<p>On the whole the British successfully managed the economics of the war. There had been no prewar plan for mobilization of economic resources. Controls were imposed slowly, as one urgent need followed another.<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup>  With the City of London the world\'s financial capital, it was possible to handle finances smoothly; in all Britain spent 4 million pounds everyday on the war effort.<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The economy (in terms of GDP) grew about 14% from 1914 to 1918 despite the absence of so many men in the services; by contrast the German economy shrank 27%. The War saw a decline of civilian consumption, with a major reallocation to munitions. The government share of GDP soared from 8% in 1913 to 38% in 1918 (compared to 50% in 1943).<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup>  The war forced Britain to use up its financial reserves and borrow large sums from private and government creditors in the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup> Shipments of American raw materials and food allowed Britain to feed itself and its army while maintaining his productivity.  The financing was generally successful,<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup> as the City\'s strong financial position minimized the damaging effects of inflation, as opposed to much worse conditions in Germany.<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup>  Overall consumer consumption declined 18% from 1914 to 1919.<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup>  Women were available and many entered munitions factories and took other home front jobs vacated by men.<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Scotland specialized in providing manpower, ships, machinery, food (particularly fish) and money. Its shipbuilding industry expanding by a third.<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rationing">Rationing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_the_United_Kingdom_during_the_First_World_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Rationing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Rationing_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Rationing in the United Kingdom">Rationing in the United Kingdom</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:%27Breaches_of_the_Rationing_Order%27_poster.jpg" class="image"><img alt="A document says &quot;DEFENCE OF THE REALM&quot;, &quot;MINISTRY OF FOOD&quot;, &quot;BREACHES OF THE RATIONING ORDER&quot;, &quot;The undermentioned convictions have been recently obtained&quot;, and a list of various offences committed and the punishments handed out." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/%27Breaches_of_the_Rationing_Order%27_poster.jpg/220px-%27Breaches_of_the_Rationing_Order%27_poster.jpg" width="220" height="120" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/%27Breaches_of_the_Rationing_Order%27_poster.jpg/330px-%27Breaches_of_the_Rationing_Order%27_poster.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/%27Breaches_of_the_Rationing_Order%27_poster.jpg/440px-%27Breaches_of_the_Rationing_Order%27_poster.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1373" data-file-height="751" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:%27Breaches_of_the_Rationing_Order%27_poster.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A British government wartime leaflet detailing the consequences of breaking the rationing laws</div></div></div>\n<p>In line with its "<a href="/wiki/Business_as_usual_(policy)" title="Business as usual (policy)">business as usual</a>" policy, the government was initially reluctant to try to control the food markets.<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup> It fought off efforts to try to introduce minimum prices in cereal production, though relenting in the area of controlling of essential imports (sugar, meat and grains). When it did introduce changes, they were only limited in their effect. In 1916, it became illegal to consume more than two courses whilst lunching in a public eating place or more than three for dinner; fines were introduced for members of the public found feeding the <a href="/wiki/Pigeon" class="mw-redirect" title="Pigeon">pigeons</a> or stray animals.<sup id="cite_ref-beck381_68-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-beck381-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In January 1917, Germany started using <a href="/wiki/U-boats" class="mw-redirect" title="U-boats">U-boats</a> (submarines) in order to sink Allied and later neutral ships bringing food to the country in an attempt to starve Britain into surrender under their <a href="/wiki/Unrestricted_submarine_warfare" title="Unrestricted submarine warfare">unrestricted submarine warfare</a> programme. One response to this threat was to introduce voluntary <a href="/wiki/Rationing" title="Rationing">rationing</a> in <span class="nowrap">February 1917</span>,<sup id="cite_ref-beck381_68-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-beck381-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> a scheme said to have been endorsed by the King and Queen themselves.<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup> Bread was subsidised from September that year; prompted by <a href="/wiki/Local_government" title="Local government">local authorities</a> taking matters into their own hands, compulsory rationing was introduced in stages between <span class="nowrap">December 1917</span> and <span class="nowrap">February 1918</span>,<sup id="cite_ref-beck381_68-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-beck381-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> as Britain\'s supply of wheat stores decreased to just six weeks worth.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup> It is said to have in the most part benefited the health of the country,<sup id="cite_ref-beck381_68-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-beck381-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> through the \'levelling of consumption of essential foodstuffs\'.<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> To assist with rationing, <a href="/wiki/Rationing" title="Rationing">ration books</a> were introduced on <span class="nowrap">15 July</span> 1918 for butter, margarine, lard, meat, and sugar.<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup> During the war, average calorific intake decreased only three percent, but protein intake six percent.<sup id="cite_ref-beck381_68-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-beck381-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Industry">Industry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_the_United_Kingdom_during_the_First_World_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Industry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:WWIworkersElisabethville.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Workers stand around a large forge, where a shell burns red-hot." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/WWIworkersElisabethville.jpg/220px-WWIworkersElisabethville.jpg" width="220" height="312" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/WWIworkersElisabethville.jpg/330px-WWIworkersElisabethville.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/WWIworkersElisabethville.jpg/440px-WWIworkersElisabethville.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1199" data-file-height="1699" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:WWIworkersElisabethville.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Forging steel shell cases</div></div></div>\n<p>Total British production fell by ten percent over the course of the war; there were, however, increases in certain industries such as steel.<sup id="cite_ref-beckett341_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-beckett341-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> Although Britain faced a highly contentious <a href="/wiki/Shell_Crisis_of_1915" title="Shell Crisis of 1915">Shell Crisis of 1915</a> With severe shortages of artillery shells reported from the Western Front. In 1915, a powerful new  <a href="/wiki/Ministry_of_Munitions" class="mw-redirect" title="Ministry of Munitions">Ministry of Munitions</a> under <a href="/wiki/David_Lloyd_George" title="David Lloyd George">David Lloyd George</a> was formed to control munitions production.<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Government\'s policy, according to historian and Conservative politician <a href="/wiki/J._A._R._Marriott" class="mw-redirect" title="J. A. R. Marriott">J. A. R. Marriott</a>, was that:\n</p>\n<dl><dd>no private interest was to be permitted to obstruct the service, or imperil the safety, of the State. Trade Union regulations must be suspended; employers\' profits must be limited, skilled men must fight, if not in the trenches, in the factories; man-power must be economize by the dilution of labour and the employment of women; Private factories must pass under the control of the State, and new national factories be set up. Results justified the new policy: the output was prodigious; the goods were at last delivered.<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup></dd></dl>\n<p>By <span class="nowrap">April 1915</span>, just two million rounds of shells had been sent to France; by the end of the war the figure had reached <span class="nowrap">187 million</span>,<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup> and a year\'s worth of pre-war production of light munitions could be completed in just four days by 1918.  Aircraft production in 1914 provided employment for 60,000 men and women; by 1918 British firms employed over 347,000.<sup id="cite_ref-beckett341_11-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-beckett341-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Labour">Labour</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_the_United_Kingdom_during_the_First_World_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Labour">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Industrial production of munitions was a central feature of the war, and with a third of the men in the labour force moved into the military, demand was very high for industrial labour. Large numbers of women were employed temporarily.<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup> Most trade unions gave strong support to the war effort, cutting back on strikes and restrictive practices.  However the coal miners and engineers were less enthusiastic.<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup> Trade unions were encouraged as membership grew from 4.1 million in 1914 to 6.5 million in 1918, peaking at 8.3 million in 1920 before relapsing to 5.4 million in 1923.<sup id="cite_ref-R._Mitchell,_1962_p_68_146-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-R._Mitchell,_1962_p_68-146">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup>  Membership soared from 4.1 million in 1914 to 6.5 million in 1918, peaking at 8.3 million in 1920 before relapsing to 5.4 million in 1923.<sup id="cite_ref-R._Mitchell,_1962_p_68_146-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-R._Mitchell,_1962_p_68-146">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup>  In 1914, 65% of union members had been associated with the <a href="/wiki/Trades_Union_Congress" title="Trades Union Congress">Trades Union Congress</a> (TUC) rising to 77% in 1920.  Women were grudgingly admitted to the trade unions.  Looking at a union of unskilled workers, Cathy Hunt concludes its regard for women workers, "was at best inconsistent and at worst aimed almost entirely at improving and protecting working conditions for its male members."<sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup>  Labour\'s prestige had never been higher, and it systematically placed its leaders into Parliament.<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Munitions_of_War_Act_1915" title="Munitions of War Act 1915">Munitions of War Act 1915</a> followed the <a href="/wiki/Shell_Crisis_of_1915" title="Shell Crisis of 1915">Shell Crisis of 1915</a> when supplies of material to the front became a political issue. The Act forbade strikes and lockouts and replaced them with compulsory arbitration. It set up a system of controlling war industries, and established munitions tribunals that were special courts to enforce good working practices. It suspended, for the duration,  restrictive practices by trade unions. It tried to control labour mobility between jobs. The courts ruled the definition of munitions was broad enough to include textile workers and dock workers. The 1915 act was repealed in 1919, but similar legislation took effect during the Second World War.<sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>It was only as late as December 1917 that a War Cabinet Committee on Manpower was established, and the British government refrained from introducing compulsory labour direction (though 388 men were moved as part of the voluntary National Service Scheme). Belgian refugees became workers, though they were often seen as "job stealers". Likewise, the use of Irish workers, because they were exempt from conscription, was another source of resentment.<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup> Worried about the impact of the dilution of labour caused by bringing external groups into the main labour pool, workers in some areas turned to <a href="/wiki/Strike_action" title="Strike action">strike action</a>. \nThe efficiency of major industries improved markedly during the war. For example, the Singer Clydebank sewing machine factory received over 5000 government contracts, and made 303 million artillery shells, shell components, fuzes, and airplane parts, as well as grenades, rifle parts, and 361,000 horseshoes. Its labour force of 14,000 was about 70 percent female at war\'s end.<sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Energy">Energy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_the_United_Kingdom_during_the_First_World_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Energy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Energy was a critical factor for the British war effort.  Most of the energy supplies came from coal mines in Britain, where the issue was labour supply.  Critical however was the flow of oil for ships, lorries and industrial use.  There were no oil wells in Britain so everything was imported. The U.S. pumped two-thirds of the world\'s oil. In 1917, total British consumption was 827 million barrels, of which 85 percent was supplied by the United States, and 6 percent by Mexico.<sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup>  The great issue in 1917 was how many tankers would survive the German u-boats. Convoys and the construction of new tankers solved the German threat, while tight government controls guaranteed that all essential needs were covered. An Inter-Allied Petroleum Conference allocated American supplies to Britain, France and Italy.<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Fuel oil for the Royal Navy was the highest priority.  In 1917, the Royal Navy consumed 12,500 tons a month, but had a supply of 30,000 tons a month from the <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Persian_Oil_Company" title="Anglo-Persian Oil Company">Anglo-Persian Oil Company</a>, using their oil wells in Persia.<sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Social_change">Social change</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_the_United_Kingdom_during_the_First_World_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Social change">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Women%27s_roles_in_the_World_Wars#World_War_I" class="mw-redirect" title="Women&#39;s roles in the World Wars">Women\'s roles in the World Wars \xc2\xa7&#160;World War I</a></div>\n<p>Variously throughout the war, serious shortage of <a href="/wiki/Able-bodied" title="Able-bodied">able-bodied</a> men ("manpower") occurred in the country, and women were required to take on many of the traditional male roles, particularly in the area of arms manufacture; though this was only significant in the later years of the war, since unemployed men were often prioritised by employers.<sup id="cite_ref-beckett455_12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-beckett455-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> Women both found work in the <a href="/wiki/Munitions" class="mw-redirect" title="Munitions">munitions</a> factories (as "munitionettes") despite initial <a href="/wiki/Trade_union" title="Trade union">trade union</a> opposition, which directly helped the war effort, but also in the <a href="/wiki/Her_Majesty%27s_Civil_Service" class="mw-redirect" title="Her Majesty&#39;s Civil Service">Civil Service</a>, where they took men\'s jobs, releasing them for the front. The number of women employed by the service increased from 33,000 in 1911 to over 102,000 by 1921.<sup id="cite_ref-Homefront_157-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Homefront-157">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup> The overall increase in female employment is estimated at <span class="nowrap">1.4 million</span>, from 5.9 to <span class="nowrap">7.3 million</span>,<sup id="cite_ref-beckett455_12-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-beckett455-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> and female trade union membership increased from 357,000 in 1914 to over a million by 1918\xe2\x80\x94an increase of 160 percent.<sup id="cite_ref-Homefront_157-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Homefront-157">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup> Beckett suggests that most of these were <a href="/wiki/Working_class" title="Working class">working class</a> women going into work at a younger age than they would otherwise have done, or married women returning to work.<sup id="cite_ref-beckett455_12-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-beckett455-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> This taken together with the fact that only 23 percent of women in the munitions industry were actually doing men\'s jobs, would limit substantially the overall impact of the war on the long-term prospects of the working woman.<sup id="cite_ref-beckett455_12-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-beckett455-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Women%27s-Land-Army-1917.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Women%27s-Land-Army-1917.jpg/220px-Women%27s-Land-Army-1917.jpg" width="220" height="335" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/80/Women%27s-Land-Army-1917.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="241" data-file-height="367" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Women%27s-Land-Army-1917.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>First World War poster</div></div></div>\n<p>When the government targeted women early in the war focussed on extending their existing roles&#160;\xe2\x80\x93 helping with Belgian refugees, for example\xe2\x80\x94but also on improving recruitment rates amongst men. They did this both through the so-called "Order of the White Feather" and through the promise of home comforts for the men while they were at the front. In <span class="nowrap">February 1916</span>, groups were set up and a campaign started to get women to help in agriculture and in <span class="nowrap">March 1917</span>, the <a href="/wiki/Women%27s_Land_Army" title="Women&#39;s Land Army">Women\'s Land Army</a> was set up. One goal was to attract middle-class women who would act as models for patriotic engagement in nontraditional duties. However the uniform of the Women\'s Land Army included male overalls and trousers, which sparked debate on the propriety of such cross-dressing. The government responded with rhetoric that explicitly feminized the new roles.<sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup>  In 1918, the <a href="/wiki/Board_of_Trade" title="Board of Trade">Board of Trade</a> estimated that there were 148,000 women in agricultural employment, though a figure of nearly 260,000 has also been suggested.<sup id="cite_ref-beckett455_12-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-beckett455-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The war also caused a split in the <a href="/wiki/Women%27s_suffrage_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Women&#39;s suffrage in the United Kingdom">British suffragette movement</a>, with the mainstream, represented by <a href="/wiki/Emmeline_Pankhurst" title="Emmeline Pankhurst">Emmeline Pankhurst</a> and her daughter <a href="/wiki/Christabel_Pankhurst" title="Christabel Pankhurst">Christabel</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/Women%27s_Social_and_Political_Union" title="Women&#39;s Social and Political Union">Women\'s Social and Political Union</a>, calling a \'<a href="/wiki/Ceasefire" title="Ceasefire">ceasefire</a>\' in their campaign for the duration of the war. In contrast, more <a href="/wiki/Extremism" title="Extremism">radical</a> suffragettes, like the <a href="/wiki/Women%27s_Suffrage_Federation" class="mw-redirect" title="Women&#39;s Suffrage Federation">Women\'s Suffrage Federation</a> run by Emmeline\'s other daughter, <a href="/wiki/Sylvia_Pankhurst" title="Sylvia Pankhurst">Sylvia</a>, continued their (at times violent) struggle. Women were also allowed to join the armed forces in a non-combatant role<sup id="cite_ref-beckett455_12-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-beckett455-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> and by the end of the War 80,000 women had joined the armed forces in auxiliary roles such as nursing and cooking.<sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Following the war, millions of returning soldiers were still not entitled to vote.<sup id="cite_ref-Hugh_160-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hugh-160">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup> This posed another dilemma for politicians since they could be seen to be withholding the vote from the very men who had just fought to preserve the British democratic political system. The <a href="/wiki/Representation_of_the_People_Act_1918" title="Representation of the People Act 1918">Representation of the People Act 1918</a> attempted to solve the problem, enfranchising all adult males as long as they were over 21 years old and were resident householders.<sup id="cite_ref-Hugh_160-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hugh-160">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup> It also gave the vote to women over 30 who met minimum property qualifications. The enfranchisement of this latter group was accepted as recognition of the contribution made by women defence workers,<sup id="cite_ref-Hugh_160-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hugh-160">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup> though the actual feelings of <a href="/wiki/Members_of_parliament#United_Kingdom" class="mw-redirect" title="Members of parliament">members of parliament</a> (MPs) at the time is questioned.<sup id="cite_ref-beckett455_12-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-beckett455-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> In the same year the <a href="/wiki/Parliament_(Qualification_of_Women)_Act_1918" title="Parliament (Qualification of Women) Act 1918">Parliament (Qualification of Women) Act 1918</a> allowed women over 21 to stand as MPs.<sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The new coalition government of 1918 charged itself with the task of creating a "land fit for heroes", from a speech given in <a href="/wiki/Wolverhampton" title="Wolverhampton">Wolverhampton</a> by David Lloyd George on <span class="nowrap">23 November</span> 1918, where he stated "What is our task? To make Britain a fit country for heroes to live in."<sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup> More generally, the war has been credited, both during and after the conflict, with removing some of the social barriers that had pervaded Victorian and Edwardian Britain.<sup id="cite_ref-social_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-social-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Regional_conditions">Regional conditions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_the_United_Kingdom_during_the_First_World_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Regional conditions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>The War had a profound influence upon rural areas, as the U-boat blockade required the government to take full control of the food chain, as well as agricultural labour. Cereal production was a high priority, and the <a href="/wiki/Corn_Production_Act_1917" title="Corn Production Act 1917">Corn Production Act 1917</a> guaranteed prices, regulated wage rates, and required farmers to meet efficiency standards.  The government campaigned heavily for turning marginal land into cropland.<sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup>  The <a href="/wiki/Women%27s_Land_Army" title="Women&#39;s Land Army">Women\'s Land Army</a> brought in 23,000 young women from the towns and cities to milk cows, pick fruit and otherwise replace the men who joined the services.<sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup> More extensive use of tractors and machinery also replaced farm labourers. However, there was a  shortage of both men and horses on the land by late 1915. County <a href="/wiki/War_Agricultural_Executive_Committee" title="War Agricultural Executive Committee">War Agricultural Executive Committees</a> reported that the continued removal of men was undercutting food production because of the farmers\' belief that operating a farm required a set number of men and horses.<sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167">&#91;167&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Kenneth_O._Morgan" title="Kenneth O. Morgan">Kenneth Morgan</a> argues that, "the overwhelming mass of the Welsh people cast aside their political and industrial divisions and threw themselves into the war with gusto." Intellectuals and ministers actively promoted the war spirit.  With 280,000 men  enrolled in the services (14% of the population), the proportionate effort in Wales outstripped both England and Scotland.<sup id="cite_ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup> However Adrian Gregory points out that the Welsh coal miners, while officially supporting the war effort, refused the government request to cut short their vacation time. After some debate, the miners agreed to extend the working day.<sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Scotland\'s distinctive  characteristics have attracted significant attention from scholars.<sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170">&#91;170&#93;</a></sup> Daniel Coetzee shows it supported the war effort with widespread enthusiasm.<sup id="cite_ref-171" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-171">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Casualties">Casualties</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_the_United_Kingdom_during_the_First_World_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Casualties">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/World_War_I_casualties" title="World War I casualties">World War I casualties</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:National_Fund_for_Welsh_Troops2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f4/National_Fund_for_Welsh_Troops2.jpg/220px-National_Fund_for_Welsh_Troops2.jpg" width="220" height="336" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f4/National_Fund_for_Welsh_Troops2.jpg/330px-National_Fund_for_Welsh_Troops2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f4/National_Fund_for_Welsh_Troops2.jpg/440px-National_Fund_for_Welsh_Troops2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2557" data-file-height="3906" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:National_Fund_for_Welsh_Troops2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Poster for a fundraising event in support of Welsh troops by <a href="/wiki/Frank_Brangwyn" title="Frank Brangwyn">Frank Brangwyn</a></div></div></div>\n<p>In the post war publication <i>Statistics of the Military Effort of the British Empire During the Great War 1914\xe2\x80\x931920</i> (<a href="/wiki/War_Office" title="War Office">The War Office</a>, <span class="nowrap">March 1922</span>), the official report lists 908,371 \'soldiers\' as being either <a href="/wiki/Killed_in_action" title="Killed in action">killed in action</a>, dying of wounds, dying as <a href="/wiki/Prisoner_of_war" title="Prisoner of war">prisoners of war</a> or missing in action in the World War. (This is broken down into Britain and its colonies 704,121; British India 64,449; Canada 56,639; Australia 59,330; New Zealand 16,711; South Africa 7,121.)<sup id="cite_ref-WAR_15-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WAR-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> Listed separately were the Royal Navy (including the Royal Naval Air Service until 31 March 1918) war dead and missing of 32,287 and the <a href="/wiki/Merchant_Navy" class="mw-redirect" title="Merchant Navy">Merchant Navy</a> war dead of 14,661.<sup id="cite_ref-WAR_15-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WAR-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> The figures for the Royal Flying Corps and the nascent Royal Air Force were not given in the War Office report.<sup id="cite_ref-WAR_15-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WAR-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>A second publication, <i>Casualties and Medical Statistics</i> (1931), the final volume of the <i>Official Medical History of the War</i>, gives British Empire Army losses by cause of death.<sup id="cite_ref-Mitchell_16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mitchell-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> The total losses in combat from 1914 to 1918 were 876,084, which included 418,361 killed, 167,172 died of wounds, 113,173 died of disease or injury, 161,046 missing presumed dead and 16,332 died as a prisoner of war.<sup id="cite_ref-Mitchell_16-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mitchell-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_War_Graves_Commission" title="Commonwealth War Graves Commission">Commonwealth War Graves Commission</a> lists 888,246 war dead from the UK and colonies (excluding the <a href="/wiki/Dominion" title="Dominion">dominions</a>, which are listed separately). This figure includes identified burials and those commemorated by name on memorials; there are an additional 187,644 unidentified burials from the Commonwealth (then Empire) as a whole.<sup id="cite_ref-cwgc.org_172-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cwgc.org-172">&#91;172&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The civilian death rate exceeded the prewar level by 292,000, which included 109,000 deaths due to food shortages and 183,577 from <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Flu" class="mw-redirect" title="Spanish Flu">Spanish Flu</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-WAR_15-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WAR-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> The 1922 War Office report detailed the deaths of 1,260 civilians and 310 military personnel due to air and sea bombardment the home islands.<sup id="cite_ref-173" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-173">&#91;173&#93;</a></sup> Losses at sea were 908 civilians and 63 fisherman killed by <a href="/wiki/U-boat" title="U-boat">U-boat</a> attacks.<sup id="cite_ref-174" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-174">&#91;174&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>With a population of 4.8 million in 1911, Scotland sent 690,000 men to the war, of whom 74,000 died in combat or from disease, and 150,000 were seriously wounded.<sup id="cite_ref-175" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-175">&#91;175&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-176" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-176">&#91;176&#93;</a></sup>  At times Scottish troops made up large proportions of the active combatants, and suffered corresponding loses, as at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Loos" title="Battle of Loos">Battle of Loos</a>, where there were three full Scots divisions and other Scottish units.<sup id="cite_ref-Lenman&amp;Mackie1991_89-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lenman&amp;Mackie1991-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> Thus, although Scots were only 10 per cent of the British population, they made up 15 per cent of the national armed forces and eventually accounted for 20 per cent of the dead.<sup id="cite_ref-Buchanan2003p49_177-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Buchanan2003p49-177">&#91;177&#93;</a></sup> Some areas, like the thinly populated Island of <a href="/wiki/Lewis_and_Harris" title="Lewis and Harris">Lewis and Harris</a> suffered some of the highest proportional losses of any part of Britain.<sup id="cite_ref-Lenman&amp;Mackie1991_89-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lenman&amp;Mackie1991-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> Clydeside shipyards and the engineering shops of west-central Scotland became the most significant centre of shipbuilding and arms production in the Empire. In the Lowlands, particularly Glasgow, poor working and living conditions led to industrial and political unrest.<sup id="cite_ref-Buchanan2003p49_177-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Buchanan2003p49-177">&#91;177&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy_and_memory">Legacy and memory</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_the_United_Kingdom_during_the_First_World_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Legacy and memory">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303569">@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti>.thumbinner{width:100%!important;max-width:none!important}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:none!important;width:100%!important;text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:304px;max-width:304px"><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:302px;max-width:302px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:British_Empire_in_1898.png" class="image"><img alt="A map showing various areas highlighted, including Great Britain, Canada, South Africa, Australia and New Zealand" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/British_Empire_in_1898.png/300px-British_Empire_in_1898.png" width="300" height="131" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/British_Empire_in_1898.png/450px-British_Empire_in_1898.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/British_Empire_in_1898.png/600px-British_Empire_in_1898.png 2x" data-file-width="1434" data-file-height="624" /></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">The extent of the British Empire in 1898</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:302px;max-width:302px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:British_Empire_in_1914.png" class="image"><img alt="The same map, with additional areas coloured (see text)" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/British_Empire_in_1914.png/300px-British_Empire_in_1914.png" width="300" height="137" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/British_Empire_in_1914.png/450px-British_Empire_in_1914.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/British_Empire_in_1914.png/600px-British_Empire_in_1914.png 2x" data-file-width="1357" data-file-height="620" /></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">The extent of the British Empire in 1914</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:302px;max-width:302px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:British_Empire_1921.png" class="image"><img alt="The same map, with additional areas coloured (see text)" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/British_Empire_1921.png/300px-British_Empire_1921.png" width="300" height="132" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/British_Empire_1921.png/450px-British_Empire_1921.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/British_Empire_1921.png/600px-British_Empire_1921.png 2x" data-file-width="1425" data-file-height="625" /></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">The extent of the British Empire in 1921</div></div></div></div>\n<p>The horrors of the Western Front as well as Gallipoli and Mesopotamia were seared into the collective consciousness of the twentieth century.  To a large extent the understanding of the war in popular culture focused on the first day of the Battle of the Somme.  Historian <a href="/wiki/A._J._P._Taylor" title="A. J. P. Taylor">A. J. P. Taylor</a> argued, "The Somme set the picture by which future generations saw the First World War: brave helpless soldiers; blundering obstinate generals; nothing achieved."<sup id="cite_ref-178" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-178">&#91;178&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Images of trench warfare became iconic symbols of human suffering and endurance. The post-war world had many veterans who were maimed or damaged by shell shock. In 1921 1,187,450 men were in receipt of pensions for war disabilities, with a fifth of these having suffered serious loss of limbs or eyesight, paralysis or lunacy.<sup id="cite_ref-179" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-179">&#91;179&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The war was a major economic catastrophe as Britain went from being the world\'s largest overseas investor to being its biggest debtor, with interest payments consuming around 40 percent of the national budget.<sup id="cite_ref-inflation_180-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-inflation-180">&#91;180&#93;</a></sup> Inflation more than doubled between 1914 and its peak in 1920, while the value of the Pound Sterling fell by 61.2 percent. Reparations in the form of free German coal depressed the local industry, precipitating the <a href="/wiki/UK_General_Strike_1926" class="mw-redirect" title="UK General Strike 1926">1926 General Strike</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-inflation_180-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-inflation-180">&#91;180&#93;</a></sup> During the war British private investments abroad were sold, raising <span class="nowrap">\xc2\xa3550 million</span>. However, <span class="nowrap">\xc2\xa3250 million</span> new investment also took place during the war. The net financial loss was therefore approximately <span class="nowrap">\xc2\xa3300 million</span>; less than two years investment compared to the pre-war average rate and more than replaced by 1928.<sup id="cite_ref-181" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-181">&#91;181&#93;</a></sup> Material loss was "slight": the most significant being 40 percent of the British merchant fleet sunk by German U-boats. Most of this was replaced in 1918 and all immediately after the war.<sup id="cite_ref-182" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-182">&#91;182&#93;</a></sup> The military historian <a href="/wiki/Correlli_Barnett" title="Correlli Barnett">Correlli Barnett</a> has argued that "in objective truth the Great War in no way inflicted crippling economic damage on Britain" but that the war only "crippled the British <i>psychologically</i>" (emphasis in original).<sup id="cite_ref-183" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-183">&#91;183&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Less concrete changes include the growing assertiveness of the <a href="/wiki/British_Dominions" class="mw-redirect" title="British Dominions">Dominions</a> within the British Empire. Battles such as <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Gallipoli" class="mw-redirect" title="Battle of Gallipoli">Gallipoli</a> for Australia and New Zealand,<sup id="cite_ref-beaumont_184-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-beaumont-184">&#91;184&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Vimy_Ridge" title="Battle of Vimy Ridge">Vimy Ridge</a> for Canada led to increased national pride and a greater reluctance to remain subordinate to London.<sup id="cite_ref-pierce_18-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pierce-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> These battles were often portrayed favourably in these nations\' propaganda as symbolic of their power during the war.<sup id="cite_ref-pierce_18-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pierce-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-beaumont_184-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-beaumont-184">&#91;184&#93;</a></sup> The war released pent-up indigenous <a href="/wiki/Nationalism" title="Nationalism">nationalism</a>, as populations tried to take advantage of the precedent set by the introduction of <a href="/wiki/Self-determination" title="Self-determination">self-determination</a> in eastern Europe. Britain was to face unrest in Ireland (1919\xe2\x80\x9321), India (1919), Egypt (1919\xe2\x80\x9323), Palestine (1920\xe2\x80\x9321) and Iraq (1920) at a time when they were supposed to be demilitarising.<sup id="cite_ref-Beckett564_17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Beckett564-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> Nevertheless, Britain\'s only territorial loss came in Ireland,<sup id="cite_ref-Beckett564_17-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Beckett564-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> where the delay in finding a resolution to the <a href="/wiki/Home_rule" title="Home rule">home rule</a> issue, along with the 1916 <a href="/wiki/Easter_Rising" title="Easter Rising">Easter Rising</a> and a failed attempt to introduce <a href="/wiki/Conscription_Crisis_of_1918" title="Conscription Crisis of 1918">conscription in Ireland</a>, increased support for separatist radicals, and led indirectly to the outbreak of the <a href="/wiki/Irish_War_of_Independence" title="Irish War of Independence">Irish War of Independence</a> in 1919.<sup id="cite_ref-185" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-185">&#91;185&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Further change came in 1919. With the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Versailles" title="Treaty of Versailles">Treaty of Versailles</a>, London took charge of an additional 1,800,000 square miles (4,700,000&#160;km<sup>2</sup>) and <span class="nowrap">13 million</span> new subjects.<sup id="cite_ref-186" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-186">&#91;186&#93;</a></sup> The colonies of Germany and the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a> were distributed to the Allied powers (and to Australia, New Zealand and South Africa) as <a href="/wiki/League_of_Nations_mandate" title="League of Nations mandate">League of Nations mandates</a>, with the United Kingdom at least gaining control of <a href="/wiki/British_Mandate_for_Palestine_(legal_instrument)" title="British Mandate for Palestine (legal instrument)">Palestine and Transjordan</a>, <a href="/wiki/British_Mandate_of_Mesopotamia" class="mw-redirect" title="British Mandate of Mesopotamia">Iraq</a>, parts of <a href="/wiki/Cameroons" class="mw-redirect" title="Cameroons">Cameroon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Togo" title="Togo">Togo</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Tanganyika" title="Tanganyika">Tanganyika</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-187" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-187">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup> Indeed, the British Empire reached its territorial peak after the settlement.<sup id="cite_ref-Beckett564_17-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Beckett564-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_the_United_Kingdom_during_the_First_World_War&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="navigation" aria-label="Portals" class="noprint portal plainlist tright" style="margin:0.5em 0 0.5em 1em;border:solid #aaa 1px">\n<ul style="display:table;box-sizing:border-box;padding:0.1em;max-width:175px;background:#f9f9f9;font-size:85%;line-height:110%;font-style:italic;font-weight:bold">\n<li style="display:table-row"><span
